كتاب البيوع
اذا اسقطا الخيار أو شرطاه بعد العقد ثم اذا صح يلتحق باصل العقد لان وصف الشئ يقوم به لا بنفسه بخلاف حط الكل لا به تبديل لا صله لا تغيير لوصفه فلا يلتحق به وعلى اعتبار الالتحاق لا تكون الزيادة عوضا عن ملكه ويظهر حكم الالتحاق في التولية والمرابحة حتى يجوز على الكل في الريادة يباشر على الباقي في الحظ وفي الشفعة حتى ياخذ بما بقى في الحط وانما كان للشفيع ان ياخذ بدون الزيادة لما في الزيادة من ابطال حقه الثابت فلا يملكانه ثم الزيادة لا تصح بعد هلاك المبيع على ظاهر الرواية لان المبيع لم يبق على حالة يصح الاعتياض عنه والشئ يثبت ثم يستند بخلاف الحط لانه بحال يمكن اخراج البدل عما يقابله فيلتحق باصل العقد استنادا
ترجمہ اور مشتری کے لیے جائز ہے کہ بائع کے واسطے ثمن میں اضافہ کر دے اور بائع کے لیے جائز ہے کہ مشتری کے واسلے مبیع میں اضافہ کر دے اور یہ بھی جائز ہے کہ ثمن میں سے کم کر دے اور ان سب کے ساتھ استحقاق متعلق ہوگا نہیں اضافہ کرنا اور کم کرنا ہمارے نزدیک اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق ہو جاتے ہیں اور امام شافعی اور امام زفر کے نزدیک لاحق کرنے کے اعتبار پر صحیح نہیں ہے بلکہ ابتدائے صلہ کے اعتبار پر ہے۔ امام شافعی اور امام زفر کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ زیادتی کو ثمن قرار دے کر صحیح کہنا ممکن نہیں ہے کیونکہ مشتری کی ملک مشتری کے عوض ہو جائے گی اس لیے اصل عقد سے الحاق نہ ہوگا۔ اور اسی طرح کم کرنا، کیونکہ پورا ثمن پوری بیع کے مقابلہ میں ہو گیا ہے اس لیے اس سے نکالنا ممکن نہیں ہے پس یہ ابتداء احسان ہو گیا۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ بائع اور مشتری کم کرنے اور بڑھانے سے عقد کو ایک صفت مشروع سے دوسرے وصف مشروع کی طرف بدلتے ہیں اور وہ بیع کا نفع بخش یا نقصان دہ یا برابر ہونا ہے اور بائع اور مشتری کو عقد بیع فسخ کر دینے کا اختیار ہے تو ان دونوں کو متغیر کر دینے کا اختیار بدرجہ اولیٰ ہوگا اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ جب دونوں نے خیار ساقط کر دیا یا عقد کے بعد دونوں نے خیار کو شرط کیا۔ پھر جب یہ تغیر صحیح ہوا تو اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق کرنے کے اعتبار پر زیادتی اپنی ملک کے عوض نہ ہوگی اور اصل عقد کے ساتھ ملنے کا حکم بیع تولیہ اور بیع مرابحہ میں ظاہر ہو گا حتی کہ زیادہ کرنے کی صورت میں کل پر (مرابحہ اور تولیہ) جائز ہے اور کم کرنے کی صورت میں باقی پر (مرابحہ اور تولیہ) کرے اور شفعہ کی صورت میں بھی ظاہر ہو گا حتی کہ کم کرنے کی صورت میں ما بقی کے عوض لے گا اور شفیع کے لیے جائز ہے کہ بغیر زیادتی کے لے۔ کیونکہ زیادتی کی صورت میں شفیع کے اس حق کو باطل کرنا لازم آتا ہے جو ثابت ہے پس بائع اور مشتری اس کے مالک نہ ہوں گے۔ پھر بیع ہلاک ہونے کے بعد زیادہ کرنا ظاہر الروایہ پر جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ مبیع ایسی حالت پر باقی نہیں کہ اس کا عوض لینا صحیح ہو حالانکہ شے پہلے ثابت ہوتی ہے پھر منسوب ہوتی ہے۔ بر خلاف کم کرنے کے کیونکہ کم کرنے کی ایسی حالت ہے کہ بدل کو اس کے مقابل سے نکالنا ممکن ہے اس لیے وہ اصل عقد کی جانب منسوب ہو کر لاحق ہو جائے گی۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مشتری بائع کے لیے ثمن میں کچھ اضافہ کر دے تو جائز ہے مثلاً ایک غلام ایک سو روپیہ میں خریدا پھر مشتری نے دس روپیہ کا اضافہ کر دیا تو یہ اضافہ کرنا جائز ہے اسی طرح اگر بائع مبیع میں اضافہ کر دے یا ثمن میں سے کچھ کم کر دے تو بھی جائز ہے مثلاً ایک شخص نے ایک سو روپیہ کے عوض ایک من گندم فروخت کیا پھر اس میں دس کلو گندم کا اضافہ کر دیا یا ایک سو روپیہ ثمن میں سے کم کر دیے تو جائز ہے۔ اور استحقاق اصل اور اضافہ شدہ دونوں کے ساتھ متعلق ہو گا چنانچہ ثمن کے اندر اضافہ کی صورت میں بائع کو مبیع روکنے کا حق اس وقت تک حاصل رہے گا جب تک کہ وہ اصل ثمن اور اضافہ شدہ وصول نہ کرلے اور اسی طرح مشتری کو مبیع کے مطالبہ کا اختیار اس وقت تک نہیں ہوگا جب تک کہ وہ مزید اور مزید علیہ یعنی اصل ثمن اور اضافہ شدہ بائع کے سپرد نہ کر دے اور ثمن کم کرنے کی صورت میں مشتری ما بقی ثمن سپرد کرنے کے بعد پوری مبیع کے مطالبہ کا حقدار ہے۔ اور مبیع مستحق ہو گئی یعنی کسی نے استحقاق کا دعوی کر کے بیع مشتری سے لے لی تو ثمن کے اندر اضافہ کرنے کی صورت میں مشتری بائع سے اصل ثمن اور اضافہ ثمن دونوں واپس لے گا اور ثمن کم کرنے کی صورت میں ما بقی ثمن واپس لے گا۔
حاصل یہ کہ ہمارے نزدیک ثمن میں زیادتی کرنا اور کمی کرنا اصل عقد کے ساتھ مل جاتے ہیں گویا اصل عقد اسی زیادتی یا کمی پر واقع ہوا تھا اور امام زفر اور امام شافعی کے نزدیک زیادتی اور کمی کو اصل عقد کے ساتھ ملا کر اعتبار کرنا صحیح نہیں ہے بلکہ ثمن کے اندر زیادتی مشتری کی طرف سے ابتداء احسان کرنا اور ہبہ کرنا ہوگا یعنی عقد بیع کے بعد گویا از سر نو احسان کیا گیا ہے اور مشتری کی طرف سے بائع کے لیے ہبہ کیا گیا ہے اور صحت ہبہ کے لیے چونکہ قبضہ شرط ہے اس لیے مقدار زائد پر جب بائع کا قبضہ ہو جائے گا تب یہ ہبہ صحیح ہوگا۔ اور اگر مبیع کے اندر کچھ زیادہ کیا گیا تو یہ بائع کی طرف سے ابتداء ہبہ ہوگا اور اگر بائع نے ثمن میں سے کچھ کم کر دیا تو یہ بائع کی طرف سے مشتری کو ثمن کے ایک حصہ سے بری کر دینا ہو گا چنانچہ مشتری اگر اس کو رد کر دے تو رد ہو جائیگا یعنی اگر مشتری ثمن کے کم کرنے کو قبول نہ کرے بلکہ اس کو واپس کر دے تو بائع کے کم کرنے سے کم نہ ہوگا۔ بہر حال ان دونوں حضرات کے نزدیک زیادتی اور کمی اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق نہیں ہوتے بلکہ زیادتی از سر نو احسان اور ہبہ ہے اور کم کرنا ثمن کی ایک مقدار سے بری کرنا ہے۔ امام زفر اور امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مقدار زائد کو ثمن قرار دے کر صحیح کہنا ممکن نہیں ہے کیونکہ جب ایک شخص نے ایک غلام ایک سو روپیہ کے عوض خریدا تو یہ غلام ایک سو روپیہ کے عوض مشتری کی ملک میں داخل ہو گیا یعنی ایک سو روپیہ میں مشتری اس غلام کا مالک ہو گیا پس اب اگر مشتری نے ثمن میں زیادتی کی مثلاً دس روپیہ کا اضافہ کیا اور اس اضافہ کو اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق کیا تو گویا مشتری کی ملک یعنی اضافه شدہ ثمن خود مشتری کی ملک یعنی بیع کے عوض ہو گئی حالانکہ ایک آدمی کی ملک کا خود اسی کی ملک کے عوض ہونا نا جائز ہے اس لیے زیادہ کی ہوئی رقم اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق نہ کی جائیگی۔ اسی طرح ثمن عقد بیع کے بعد بائع کی ملک میں داخل ہو گیا۔ اب اگر بائع مبیع میں زیادتی کرے گا تو بائع کی ملک یعنی مقدار زائد خود بائع کی ملک یعنی ثمن کے عوض ہو جائے گی حالانکہ یہ نا جائز ہے اس لیے مبیع کی مقدار زائد اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق نہ ہوگی۔ اسی طرح ثمن میں سے کچھ کم کرنا اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق نہیں کیا جائیگا کیونکہ پورا ثمن پوری مبیع کے مقابلہ میں ہے یعنی ایک سو روپیہ ایک غلام کے مقابلہ میں ہے پس اب اگر مثال دس روپے کم کر دیئے گئے اور اس کو اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق کر دیا گیا تو مبیع یعنی غلام کا ایک حصہ بغیر عوض کے رہ جائیگا حالانکہ یہ نا جائز ہے کیونکہ بیع کا کوئی حصہ بغیر عوض نہیں ہوتا اس لیے ثمن میں سے کچھ کم کرنا ممکن نہیں ہے اور کم کرنے کے بعد باقی ثمن یعنی نوے روپیہ کو پوری مبیع کے مقابل قرار دینا بھی ممکن نہیں ہے کیونکہ ایسا کوئی عقد جدید نہیں پایا گیا جس میں نوے روپیہ کو ثمن ٹھہرایا گیا ہو۔ پس جب زیادتی اور کمی کو اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق کرنا ممکن نہیں پایا گیا تو زیادتی کی صورت میں مقدار زائد کو از سرنو ہبہ اور احسان قرار دیا جائے گا اور کمی کی صورت میں کم کی ہوئی مقدار کو ابراء یعنی بری کر دینا شمار کیا جائے گا۔
ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ بیع تین طرح پر مشروع کی گئی ہے۔
Book of Sales. If they drop the option or stipulate it after the contract, then when it is valid, it attaches to the original contract because the description of a thing is based on it, not on itself, contrary to the reduction of the whole which is not a substitution, not a connection, not a change of its description, so it does not attach. And on the basis of attachment, the increase is not a substitute for his ownership. And the ruling of attachment appears in tawliyah and murabahah, so that it is permissible on the whole in the increase, and he proceeds on the remainder in the reduction. And in shuf'a, he takes what remains in the reduction. And the pre-emptor may take without the increase because in the increase there is a nullification of his established right, so they do not own it. Then the increase is not valid after the destruction of the sold object according to the apparent narration because the sold object did not remain in a state in which taking compensation for it is valid, and the thing is established then attributed, contrary to the reduction because it is in a state in which it is possible to extract the substitute from what corresponds to it, so it attaches to the original contract by attribution.
Translation: And it is permissible for the buyer to increase the price for the seller, and it is permissible for the seller to increase the object of sale for the buyer, and it is also permissible to decrease the price. And all of these are connected to entitlement. Increasing and decreasing according to us attach to the original contract, and according to Imam Shafi'i and Imam Zufar, they are not valid on the basis of attachment, but rather on the basis of a new gift. Their evidence is that it is not possible to validly consider the increase as price because the buyer's ownership would become in exchange for his own ownership, so it cannot attach to the original contract. Similarly, decreasing because the entire price is in exchange for the entire sale, so it is not possible to remove from it, so it becomes a new gift. Our evidence is that the seller and buyer, by decreasing and increasing, change the contract from one prescribed description to another prescribed description, and that is the sale being profitable, loss-making, or equal, and the seller and buyer have the authority to rescind the sale contract, so they have a fortiori authority to modify it. And this is like when they drop the option or stipulate the option after the contract. Then when this modification is valid, on the basis of attachment to the original contract, the increase is not in exchange for his own ownership, and the ruling of joining with the original contract appears in tawliyah and murabahah, so that in the case of increase, it is permissible on the whole, and in the case of decrease, it is permissible on the remainder in tawliyah and murabahah. And it also appears in the case of shuf'a, so that in the case of decrease, he takes for what remains, and it is permissible for the pre-emptor to take without the increase because the increase would necessitate nullifying his established right, so the seller and buyer do not own it. Then after the destruction of the sale, increase is not valid according to the apparent narration because the sold object did not remain in a state in which taking compensation for it is valid, although the thing is established first then attributed. Contrary to decreasing, because decreasing is in a state where it is possible to extract the substitute from its counterpart, so it attaches to the original contract by attribution.
Explanation: The scenario is that if the buyer increases the price for the seller, it is permissible. For example, he bought a slave for one hundred rupees, then the buyer increased by ten rupees—this increase is permissible. Similarly, if the seller increases the object of sale or decreases some of the price, it is also permissible. For example, a person sold one maund of wheat for one hundred rupees, then he increased ten kilograms of wheat or decreased ten rupees from the price—it is permissible. And entitlement attaches to both the original and the increased amount. Thus, in the case of increase in the price, the seller retains the right to withhold the sold object until he receives the original price and the increase. Similarly, the buyer does not have the right to demand the sold object until he hands over the original price and the increase to the seller. In the case of decrease in the price, the buyer is entitled to demand the entire sold object after handing over the remaining price. And if the sold object is subject to a claim (i.e., someone claimed it and took it from the buyer), then in the case of increase in the price, the buyer takes back both the original price and the increase from the seller; in the case of decrease, he takes back the remaining price.
The gist is that according to us, increasing and decreasing the price merge with the original contract as if the original contract was concluded with that increase or decrease. According to Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i, it is not valid to consider the increase and decrease merged with the original contract; rather, the increase in the price is a new gift (hiba) from the buyer, i.e., after the sale contract, it is as if a new gift was made, and the buyer gives a gift to the seller. For the validity of a gift, possession is a condition, so when the seller takes possession of the increased amount, the gift becomes valid. If something is increased in the object of sale, it is a gift from the seller. If the seller decreases some of the price, it is a discharge (ibra) by the seller to the buyer of a portion of the price. Therefore, if the buyer rejects it, it is rejected—i.e., if the buyer does not accept the decrease but returns it, the decrease does not take effect. In any case, according to these two scholars, increase and decrease do not attach to the original contract; rather, increase is a new gift, and decrease is a discharge of a portion of the price. Their evidence is that it is not possible to validly consider the increased amount as price because when a person bought a slave for one hundred rupees, the slave became owned by the buyer in exchange for one hundred rupees, i.e., the buyer owned the slave by paying one hundred rupees. Now if the buyer increases the price by ten rupees and attaches this increase to the original contract, then the buyer's property (the increased price) becomes in exchange for his own property (the slave), which is impermissible. Similarly, the price after the sale contract has become the property of the seller. If the seller increases the object of sale, then the seller's property (the increase) becomes in exchange for his own property (the price), which is impermissible. Likewise, decreasing some of the price cannot be attached to the original contract because the entire price is in exchange for the entire sold object—i.e., one hundred rupees for one slave. If you decrease ten rupees and attach it to the original contract, then a portion of the sold object (the slave) remains without consideration, which is impermissible because no part of a sale can be without consideration. It is also not possible to consider the remaining ninety rupees as the price for the entire slave because there is no new contract in which ninety rupees was agreed as the price. Since it is impossible to attach increase and decrease to the original contract, in the case of increase, the increased amount is considered a new gift, and in the case of decrease, the decreased amount is considered a discharge (ibra).
Our evidence is that sale is prescribed in three types.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the legal effect of increasing or decreasing the price (or the object of sale) after a sale contract has been concluded. The Hanafi school holds that such adjustments attach (ilhaq) to the original contract, as if the contract was originally made with the adjusted terms. The opposing view of Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Zufar is that these are separate legal acts: a gift (hiba) for an increase or a discharge (ibra) for a decrease, requiring separate conditions (e.g., possession for a gift). The text begins by stating the Hanafi ruling: it is permissible for the buyer to increase the price, for the seller to increase the object, or for either to decrease the price, and all these adjustments connect to the original contract. This is derived from a broader principle: since the parties have the power to rescind the entire contract (by mutual consent), they have a fortiori the power to modify its terms. The attachment has practical consequences in sales like tawliyah (resale at cost) and murabahah (cost-plus sale): if an increase occurred, the later sale is on the total; if a decrease, on the remainder. In pre-emption (shuf'a), the pre-emptor takes the property at the reduced price but cannot force the parties to include an increase that would cancel his right. The text also distinguishes between increase and decrease after the destruction of the sold object: increase is invalid (per the zahir al-riwaya) because the object no longer exists to be exchanged, whereas decrease remains valid because it merely involves giving up a portion of the price, which is still possible. The Hanafi reasoning is based on analogy (qiyas) with dropping or stipulating options after the contract—these attach to the original contract, so price adjustments should too. The opponents argue that an increase would mean a person's property (the extra price) is exchanged for his own property (the object he already owns), which is invalid (as one cannot sell one's own property back to oneself). Similarly, a decrease would leave part of the sold object without consideration. The Hanafi response is that the adjustment changes the 'description' (wasf) of the contract (e.g., from profitable to loss-making) rather than the essence, and the parties have authority to modify descriptions just as they have authority to rescind. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the Hanafi view, as stated in the text by 'our evidence'. The authority weight is from the zahir al-riwaya regarding the invalidity of increase after destruction, but the main ruling is established in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الزيادة (al-ziyādah): An increase in the price (thaman) or object of sale (mabi') added after the contract by mutual agreement.
الحط (al-ḥaṭṭ): A decrease in the price (thaman) after the contract, agreed between buyer and seller.
الإلحاق (al-ilḥāq): The legal attachment of a post-contract adjustment to the original contract, treating it as if it were part of the original terms.
الهبة (al-hibah): A gift; the opposing view classifies post-sale increases as a gift requiring possession.
الإبراء (al-ibrā’): A discharge; the opposing view classifies a price decrease as a discharge of part of the debt, which can be rejected by the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal effect of the buyer voluntarily increasing the price after the sale contract?
A) The increase is invalid because the buyer already owns the sold object.
B) The increase is a separate gift (hiba) requiring possession by the seller.
C) The increase attaches to the original contract, as if the contract was made with the higher price.
D) The increase is valid only if the seller agrees to a new contract.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, post-sale price modifications (increase or decrease) are not independent acts but rather adjustments that retroactively attach to the original sale contract.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Post-Sale Modifications: Annexation vs. New Acts (Ilhaq)
Inter-madhhab dispute on annexing post-contract increase/decrease to original sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi position on post-sale price/commodity modifications from the Shafi'i/Zufar position, and apply the Hanafi rule of annexation (ilhaq) to practical scenarios such as istihqaq.

Core Idea
Post-sale increase or decrease merges with the original sale; it is not a separate gift or remission.

In this lesson, we explore a key inter-madhhab dispute: when a buyer and seller agree to change the price or the commodity after the sale is concluded, does that change become part of the original contract, or is it a new independent transaction? The Hanafi school, citing the categorization of sales and the analogy of contractual options, holds that the modification is annexed (ilhaq) to the original contract. The Shafi'i school and Imam Zufar treat it as a new gift (hiba) or remission (ibra'), with different consequences for rights and obligations. Understanding this dispute clarifies not only the flexibility of the Hanafi contract but also the practical outcome when the sold item is reclaimed by a third party.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that post-sale increase or decrease is annexed to the original contract; it is as if the contract was concluded with that modification. This is the mufta bihi view.
The text says 'hamaare nazdeek' (according to us) indicating the Hanafi position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i reject annexation. They treat an increase as a new gift (hiba) that requires possession, and a decrease as a remission (ibra') that the buyer may reject.
Their view leads to different legal consequences for recovery and bindingness.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Their objection: annexing an increase would make the buyer's own property the consideration for his own property (impossible), and annexing a decrease would leave part of the sold good without consideration. No new contract exists for the reduced amount.
This is a rational (aqlī) argument based on the impossibility of certain ownership exchanges.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that annexation does not require ownership of one's own property as consideration; the increase is deemed to have existed at the time of contract. Partial reduction is a change of description (wasf), not essence, whereas full reduction would change the contract itself, hence not analogous.
The Hanafi response also invokes the analogy of khiyar (contractual options) and the three types of sale (loss-making, profitable, fair).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the sold good is reclaimed by a third party (istihqaq), a Hanafi buyer recovers the increased price; a Shafi'i buyer recovers only the original price because the increase was a gift not possessed. Also, a price reduction is binding under Hanafi law but rejectable under Shafi'i law.
This difference affects the calculation of damages and the rights of the buyer.

جواب ... اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ جب بائع نے پوراشن کم کر دیا تو اصل عقد ہی بدل گیا۔ کیونکہ جب پوراشن کم کر دیا تو شمن نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے یا تو بیع باطل ہو جائے گی حالانکہ انھوں نے بیع باطل کا ارادہ نہیں کیا ہے یا مبیع بائع کی طرف سے مشتری کے لیے ہدیہ ہو جائے گی حالانکہ ان دونوں کا مقصد مبیع کے اندر تجارت کرنا تھا نہ کہ ہدیہ کرنا۔ حاصل یہ کہ پورا من کم کر دینے میں اصل عقد کی تبدیلی ہے نہ کہ تغیر وصف اور چونکہ اصل عقد بدل جانے کی صورت میں کوئی چیز باقی ہی نہ رہی جس کے ساتھ الحاق کیا جاتا اس لیے پورا شمن کم کر دینے کو اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق نہیں کیا جائے گا اور کچھ شن کم کرنے کی صورت میں چونکہ اصل عقد باقی رہتا ہے محض اس کا وصف تبدیل ہوتا ہے اس لیے اس صورت میں شمن کم کرنے کو اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق کیا جائے گا
و على اعتبار الالتحاق سے امام زفر اور امام شافعی کی دلیل کا جواب ہے جو اب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ زیادتی کو جب اصل نہا کے ساتھ لاحق کر دیا گیا تو مشتری کی ملک کا مشتری کی ملک کے عوض ہونالازم نہیں آئے گا کیونکہ جب شمن کے اندر زیادتی اصل عقد کے ساتھ لائق ہوگئی تو ایسا ہو گیا گویا وہ عقد کے وقت موجود تھی مثلا شمن ایک سو روپیہ ہے اور مشتری نے دس روپیہ زیادہ کر دیے تو یہ
283
Book of Sales
Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary on Hidayah, Volume 8. The right of retention remains until the original price and the increase are received. Similarly, the buyer's right to demand the sold good does not exist until he hands over the original price and the increase to the seller. In case of a decrease in the price, after handing over the remaining price, the buyer is entitled to demand the entire sold good. If the sold good is claimed (by a third party) and taken from the buyer, then in case of increase in the price, the buyer takes back from the seller both the original price and the increase; in case of decrease, he takes back the remaining price. The conclusion is that according to us, increasing or decreasing the price merges with the original contract, as if the original contract was concluded with that increase or decrease. According to Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i, it is not correct to consider the increase and decrease as annexed to the original contract. Rather, an increase in the price is a gratuitous favor (ihsan) and a gift (hiba) from the buyer; i.e., after the sale contract, it is a new favor and a gift from the buyer to the seller. Since possession (qabd) is a condition for the validity of a gift, the gift becomes valid only when the seller takes possession of the increased amount. If something is increased in the sold good, it is a gift from the seller. If the seller reduces some of the price, it is a remission (ibra') from the seller to the buyer of a part of the price. Consequently, if the buyer rejects it, it is rejected; i.e., if the buyer does not accept the reduction and returns it, the reduction does not take effect. In any case, according to these two scholars, increase and decrease are not annexed to the original contract; rather, the increase is a new favor and gift, and the decrease is a remission of a part of the price. Their evidence: It is not possible to consider the increased amount as part of the price validly because when a person buys a slave for one hundred rupees, the slave enters his ownership in exchange for one hundred rupees. Now if the buyer adds ten rupees and annexes this addition to the original contract, then the buyer's property (the additional price) would become the consideration for the buyer's own property (the sold good), which is impermissible. Similarly, the price enters the seller's ownership after the contract. So if the seller increases in the sold good, the seller's property (the addition) would become the consideration for the seller's own property (the price), which is also impermissible. Similarly, decreasing some of the sold good cannot be annexed to the original contract because the entire sold good is in exchange for the entire price. If some of the price is reduced and annexed, a part of the sold good would remain without consideration, which is impermissible. Nor is it possible to consider the remaining price (ninety rupees) as the exchange for the entire sold good, because there is no new contract in which ninety rupees is stipulated as the price. Since annexation of increase and decrease to the original contract is not possible, in case of increase the extra amount is considered a new gift and favor, and in case of decrease the reduced amount is considered a remission (ibra'). Our evidence: Sale is legislated in three types:

[PAGE_285]
Book of Sales: loss-making, profitable, fair.
Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary on Hidayah, Volume 8.
Loss-making sale involves loss, profitable sale involves profit, and fair sale involves neither loss nor profit. So if the price is increased, the increase turns a loss-making sale into a fair one, and a fair sale into a profitable one. Decreasing some of the price turns a profitable sale into a fair one, and a fair sale into a loss-making one. Similarly, if something is increased in the sold good, this increase makes a loss-making sale fair in the buyer's favor, and a fair sale profitable; in the seller's favor, it makes a profitable sale fair and a fair sale loss-making. So when the seller and buyer, after the contract, mutually agree to increase or decrease the price or the sold good, it is as if they have changed the sale from one legislated description to another, i.e., from loss-making to fair, or from fair to profitable, or from profitable to fair, or from fair to loss-making. Since the seller and buyer have the power to rescind the original contract, they have the power to change the contract from one description to another a fortiori, because making a change in the description is easier than making a change in the thing itself. This is similar to a case where one of the contracting parties or both had a stipulated option (khiyar al-shart); then they both drop it, or they both stipulate an option after the contract (when there was no option originally). In the first case, the contract changes from a binding description to a non-binding one, and in the second case from binding to non-binding, and this is permissible by consensus. Just as this change is permissible, similarly the change of increase and decrease is permissible. In any case, when increasing or decreasing the price after the contract is valid, it is annexed to the original contract because increase and decrease are descriptions of the price, and a description of a thing subsists in that thing itself, not independently. Therefore, increase and decrease in the price subsist in the price, not independently. Question: But if reducing part of the price is valid, then by analogy reducing the entire price should also be valid, whereas reducing the entire price is not valid. Answer: When the seller reduces the entire price, the original contract itself changes. Because if the entire price is reduced, then due to the absence of price, either the sale becomes invalid (batil) — though they did not intend an invalid sale — or the sold good becomes a gift (hidya) from the seller to the buyer — though their purpose was trade, not a gift. The conclusion is that reducing the entire price changes the original contract itself, not just its description. Since nothing remains to which it could be annexed, reducing the entire price is not annexed to the original contract. In the case of reducing part of the price, since the original contract remains and merely its description changes, the reduction is annexed to the original contract. "And on the basis of annexation" is the answer to the evidence of Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i, the summary of which is: When the increase is annexed to the original contract, it does not necessitate that the buyer's property becomes the consideration for the buyer's own property, because when the increase in the price is annexed to the original contract, it is as if it existed at the time of the contract. For example, the price is one hundred rupees, and the buyer increased it by ten rupees. So this...

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the legal effect of a post-sale agreement to increase or decrease the price or the commodity. The Hanafi position, attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa and his disciples, is that such modifications are annexed to the original contract (ilhaq bi'l aqd al-asi), meaning they are treated as if they were part of the original agreement. This has practical consequences: for example, if the sold item is later reclaimed by a third party (istihqaq), the buyer recovers the original price plus any increase he paid, or only the remaining price if a decrease was agreed. The Hanafis base their view on the categorization of sales: loss-making (khasiroh), profitable (rabeh), and fair (adl). Changing the price or commodity shifts the sale from one valid category to another, which is permissible just as modifying a contractual option (khiyar) after the contract is permissible. They argue that increase or decrease is a description (wasf) of the price/commodity, which naturally attaches to its subject. In contrast, Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i reject annexation. They argue that after the contract, ownership has already transferred; increasing the price would mean the buyer's own property becomes the consideration for his own property (impossible), and decreasing would leave part of the commodity without consideration. Instead, they view the increase as a new gift (hiba) requiring possession, and the decrease as a remission (ibra') which the buyer may reject. The Hanafi response counters that annexation does not imply ownership exchange, as the increase is deemed to have existed at the time of contract (i.e., retrospectively). The passage also addresses a potential objection: if partial reduction is allowed, why not full reduction? The answer distinguishes between changing a description (partial reduction) and changing the essence (full reduction, which would nullify or transform the contract entirely). Thus, the Hanafi school holds that post-sale modifications are valid and annexed to the original contract, while the opposing school treats them as independent legal acts.

Key Terms
التحاق (ilhaq): The annexation of a post-contract increase or decrease to the original contract, treating it as if it were part of the original agreement.
زیادتی (ziyadati): An increase in the price or commodity after the contract, agreed upon by both parties.
کمی (kami): A decrease in the price or commodity after the contract, agreed upon by both parties.
بیع خاسر (bay' khasir): A sale where the price is less than the cost, resulting in a loss for the seller.
بیع رابج (bay' rabih): A sale where the price exceeds the cost, resulting in profit for the seller.
بیع عدل (bay' 'adl): A sale where the price equals the cost, with no loss or profit.
ابراء (ibra'): Remission or discharge of a debt; here, the seller's act of forgiving part of the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a buyer agrees to increase the price after a sale contract, what is the legal status of that increase?
A) It is a new gift (hiba) requiring separate possession.
B) It is annexed to the original contract, as if it was part of the original price.
C) It is invalid because a contract cannot be altered after conclusion.
D) It is a remission (ibra') of debt.

Key Takeaway
Post-sale modifications by mutual agreement are valid and annexed to the original contract in Hanafi law, treating them as if they were part of the original terms.

Post-Sale Price Adjustments: Validity and Annexation
Establishes permissibility via qiyas and annexation rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on post-sale price adjustments and explain the qiyas analogy with khiyar al-shart.

Core Idea
Mutual consent to change the price after a sale is valid — it is annexed to the original contract because changing a description is easier than annulment.

You already know that a sale contract is binding once concluded. But what if both parties later agree to raise or lower the price? Is that allowed? The Hanafi school says yes, and this passage from the Hidayah explains the reasoning, drawing an analogy with the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart). We will unpack the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of sale
The passage defines khasir (loss-making), rabih (profitable), and 'adl (fair). These categories depend on how the price compares to market value.
This tripartite division is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
2
Understand the effect of post-contract adjustments
Increasing the price moves the sale from khasir to 'adl or 'adl to rabih; decreasing moves it from rabih to 'adl or 'adl to khasir. The same applies to changes in the object from the buyer's and seller's perspectives.
The adjustment changes the legal description of the sale.
3
Recognize the a fortiori argument
Since the parties have the right to annul the entire contract (a more drastic act), they certainly have the right to change a mere description (the price), because dealing with a description is easier.
This is an argument from the greater to the lesser (mafhūm al-muwāfaqa).
4
Examine the qiyas with khiyar al-shart
Khiyar al-shart allows parties to stipulate or drop an option after the contract, changing the contract's binding nature. This is unanimously permissible. Similarly, changing the price changes the description and is permissible by analogy.
The illa is the ease of changing a description versus the essence.
5
Apply the rule of annexation (ilhaq)
The adjustment is not a separate contract; it is annexed (ilhaq) to the original price because the attribute (increase or decrease) belongs to the price and cannot exist independently.
Thus the adjusted price is treated as if it were specified at the time of the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو بدا یه جلد هشتم ہدایہ
بیچ خاسر میں گھانا ہوتا ہے اور بیچ رابج میں نفع ہوتا ہے اور بیج عدل میں نہ گھانا ہوتا اور نہ نفع ہوتا۔ پس اگر نشن میں زیادتی کی گئی تو زیادتی بیع خاسر کو عادل اور بیچ عادل کو راج کر دیتی ہے اور ثمن میں سے کچھ کم کر دینا بیع رایج کو عدل اور بیج عدل کو خاسر کر دیتا ہے اسی طرح اگر مبیع میں زیادتی کی گئی تو یہ زیادتی مشتری کے حق میں مبیع خاسر کو عدل اور بیع عدل کو رائج کر دیتی ہے اور بائع کے حق میں رائج کو عدل اور عدل کو خاسر کر دیتی ہے پس بائع اور مشتری نے جب عقد بیع کے بعد باہمی رضامندی سے ثمن یا مبیع میں کچھ اضافہ کیا یا کمی کی ۔ تو گویا انھوں نے اپنے عقد بیع کو ایک وصف مشروع سے دوسرے وصف مشروع کی طرف بدل دیا یعنی خاسر کو عدل یا عدل کو رائج کر دیا یا رانج کو عدل کر دیا با عدل کو خاسر کر دیا۔ اور بائع اور مشتری کو اصل عقد نسخ کر دینے کا اختیار ہے تو عقد بیع کو ایک وصف سے دوسرے وصف کی طرفی متغیر کر دینے کا اختیار بدرجہ اولی ہوگا کیونکہ وصف کے اندر تصرف کرنا اصل شئی کے اندر تصرف کرنے سے اہون اور آسان ہے اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ احد العاقدین یا عاقدین کے لیے خیار شرط تھا۔ پھر دونوں نے اس کو ساقط کر دیا یا دونوں نے عقد کے بعد خیار کو شرط کیا یعنی اصل عقد میں کسی کے واسطے خیار نہ تھا پھر عقد کے بعد دونوں نے شرط کیا تو پہلی صورت میں عقد ، صفت غیر لزوم کی طرف اور دوسری صورت میں لزوم سے غیر لزوم کی طرف متغیر ہو جاتا ہے اور یہ بالا تفاق جائز ہے پس جس طرح یہ تغیر جائز ہے اسی طرح کمی بیشی کا تغیر بھی جائز ہے بہر حال جب عقد بیع کے بعد ثمن میں زیادتی اور کمی کرنا صحیح ہے تو اس کو اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق کیا جائیگا کیونکہ یمن کے اندر زیادتی اور کمی شمن کا وصف ہے اور شئی کا وصف اسی شئی کے ساتھ قائم ہوتا ہے بذات خود قائم نہیں ہوتا اس لئے شمن کے اندر زیادتی اور کی شمن کے ساتھ قائم ہو گی بذات خود قائم نہ ہوگی۔
Book of Sales: Khasir (loss-making), Rabih (profitable), 'Adl (fair). In a khasir sale there is loss, in a rabih sale there is profit, and in an 'adl sale there is neither loss nor profit. So if the price is increased, the increase turns the khasir sale into 'adl and the 'adl sale into rabih. And decreasing some of the price turns the rabih sale into 'adl and the 'adl sale into khasir. Similarly, if there is an increase in the object of sale, this increase turns, from the buyer's perspective, a khasir sale into 'adl and an 'adl sale into rabih, and from the seller's perspective, a rabih sale into 'adl and an 'adl sale into khasir. So when the seller and the buyer, after the sale contract, by mutual consent, add something to or subtract something from the price or the object, then it is as if they changed their sale contract from one legitimate description to another, i.e., they turned khasir into 'adl or 'adl into rabih or rabih into 'adl or 'adl into khasir. And since the seller and buyer have the right to annul the original contract, they have a fortiori the right to change the sale contract from one description to another, because dealing with a description is easier and simpler than dealing with the essence of the thing. And this is like when one of the contracting parties or both have an option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart). Then they both drop it, or they both stipulate an option after the contract, i.e., there was no option for anyone in the original contract, then after the contract both stipulate it. In the first case, the contract changes from a non-binding attribute to a binding one, and in the second case from binding to non-binding, and this is unanimously permissible. So just as this change is permissible, similarly the change of increase and decrease is permissible. In any case, when increasing and decreasing the price after the sale contract is valid, then it is annexed to the original contract, because increase and decrease in the price are attributes of the price, and the attribute of a thing is established with that thing itself, not independently. Therefore, increase and decrease in the price will be established with the price, not independently.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It begins by defining three categories of sale based on the relationship between the price and the market value: loss-making (khasir), profitable (rabih), and fair ('adl). The author then explains that any consensual post-contract adjustment to the price or object changes the category. Such an adjustment is not an annulment of the original contract but a change in its description. Since the parties have the right to annul the entire contract (which is a more drastic action), they certainly have the right to change a description, this being a lesser action. This is supported by the analogy (qiyas) with khiyar al-shart: if the parties can mutually drop an existing option or add one after the contract, which also changes the description (from binding to non-binding or vice versa), then similarly they can change the description by adjusting the price. Finally, the passage establishes the legal rule of 'annexation' (ilhaq): any post-contract increase or decrease in price is annexed to the original price, becoming part of it, because the attribute follows the substance.

Key Terms
بیع خاسر (bay' khasir): A sale where the price is less than the market value, resulting in loss for the seller.
بیع رابح (bay' rabih): A sale where the price exceeds the market value, yielding profit for the seller.
بیع عَدْل (bay' 'adl): A sale at the fair market value, neither loss nor profit.
الحاق (ilhaq): Annexation: the post-contract adjustment is legally considered part of the original price or object.
خیار شرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated by one or both parties to annul or confirm the contract within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Post-sale price adjustments by mutual consent are valid in Hanafi fiqh and are annexed to the original contract, supported by an a fortiori argument and analogy with khiyar al-shart.

Objections to Post-Sale Price Changes and Their Answers
Responds to two objections: full decrease invalid and annexation leading to exchange of buyer's property for his own.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between a partial and full price decrease and defend the Hanafi ruling on annexing post-sale price increases against the Shafi'i/Zufar objection.

Core Idea
A partial post-sale price change alters a description; a full change alters the contract's essence.

After a sale contract, the parties may adjust the price. Hanafi law allows a partial reduction or increase, but not a full reduction to zero. This lesson addresses two logical objections to these rules. First, why is a full decrease not allowed by analogy from a partial decrease? Second, how can an increase be annexed without invalidating the exchange? Understanding the Hanafi answers sharpens your grasp of contract theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Read the passage and locate the two objections. The first is from an unnamed objector: if partial decrease is valid, why not full decrease by qiyas? The second is from Imam Zufar and al-Shafi'i: annexing an increase would mean the buyer exchanges his own property for his own property, which is invalid.
The first objection is about decrease, the second about increase.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response to the first objection
The Hanafi response distinguishes a change of description (partial decrease) from a change of essence (full decrease). A full decrease extinguishes the price entirely, either voiding the sale or turning it into a gift. Thus it cannot be annexed because nothing remains of the original contract.
Key point: the legal fiction of annexation (ilhaq) requires an existing contract.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the second objection
The Hanafi response explains that annexing an increase makes it retroactively part of the original price. Therefore, the exchange is between the seller's object and the buyer's total price (original + increase), not between the buyer's property and itself.
The annexation does not create a separate exchange; it integrates the increase.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in Hanafi, a post-sale price increase by the buyer is valid and annexed. In Shafi'i/Zufar's view, such an increase would require a new contract or be invalid. This affects real-world transactions where parties adjust prices after agreement.

Source Text

جواب ... اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ جب بائع نے پوراشن کم کر دیا تو اصل عقد ہی بدل گیا۔ کیونکہ جب پوراشن کم کر دیا تو شمن نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے یا تو بیع باطل ہو جائے گی حالانکہ انھوں نے بیع باطل کا ارادہ نہیں کیا ہے یا مبیع بائع کی طرف سے مشتری کے لیے ہدیہ ہو جائے گی حالانکہ ان دونوں کا مقصد مبیع کے اندر تجارت کرنا تھا نہ کہ ہدیہ کرنا۔ حاصل یہ کہ پورا من کم کر دینے میں اصل عقد کی تبدیلی ہے نہ کہ تغیر وصف اور چونکہ اصل عقد بدل جانے کی صورت میں کوئی چیز باقی ہی نہ رہی جس کے ساتھ الحاق کیا جاتا اس لیے پورا شمن کم کر دینے کو اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق نہیں کیا جائے گا اور کچھ شن کم کرنے کی صورت میں چونکہ اصل عقد باقی رہتا ہے محض اس کا وصف تبدیل ہوتا ہے اس لیے اس صورت میں شمن کم کرنے کو اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق کیا جائے گا
و على اعتبار الالتحاق سے امام زفر اور امام شافعی کی دلیل کا جواب ہے جو اب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ زیادتی کو جب اصل نہا کے ساتھ لاحق کر دیا گیا تو مشتری کی ملک کا مشتری کی ملک کے عوض ہونالازم نہیں آئے گا کیونکہ جب شمن کے اندر زیادتی اصل عقد کے ساتھ لائق ہوگئی تو ایسا ہو گیا گویا وہ عقد کے وقت موجود تھی مثلا شمن ایک سو روپیہ ہے اور مشتری نے دس روپیہ زیادہ کر دیے تو یہ
دس روپیہ جب اصل عقد کے ساتھ مل گئے تو ایسا ہو گیا گویا مشترکی نے ابتداء صبیح ایک سو دس روپیہ کے عوض خریدی ہے اور جب مشترکی نے ابتداء مبیع ایک سو دس روپیہ کے عوض خریدی تو مشتری کی ملک کا مشتری کی ملک کے عوض ہونا لازم نہیں آئے گا بلکہ مائع کی ملک یعنی مبیع مشترکی کی ملک یعنی ایک سو دس روپیہ کے عوض ہو جائے گی اور اس میں کوئی قباحت نہیں ہے۔
Question: But if it is asked: When decreasing part of the price is valid, then by analogy (qiyas) of the whole to the part, decreasing the entire price should also be valid, yet decreasing the entire price is not valid. Answer: The answer is that when the seller decreases the entire price, the original contract itself changes. Because when the entire price is decreased, due to the absence of price, either the sale becomes void (batil) although they did not intend a void sale, or the object becomes a gift (hiba) from the seller to the buyer, although their intent was trade in the object, not gifting. The upshot is that in decreasing the entire price, there is a change of the original contract, not a change of description. And since when the original contract changes, nothing remains to which annexation could be made, therefore decreasing the entire price is not annexed to the original contract. In the case of decreasing part of the price, since the original contract remains and only its description changes, therefore in this case decreasing the price is annexed to the original contract. And by way of considering annexation, there is an answer to the evidence of Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i, whose gist is: When the increase is annexed to the original contract, it does not necessarily follow that the buyer's property is exchanged for the buyer's property, because when the increase in price is annexed to the original contract, it becomes as if it was present at the time of the contract. For example, the price is one hundred rupees and the buyer adds ten rupees. These ten rupees, when joined to the original contract, become as if the buyer initially purchased the object for one hundred and ten rupees. And when the buyer initially purchased the object for one hundred and ten rupees, it does not necessitate that the buyer's property is exchanged for the buyer's property. Rather, the seller's property (i.e., the object) is exchanged for the buyer's property (i.e., one hundred and ten rupees), and there is no objection in this.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage deals with two objections to the rule of post-sale price adjustments and provides Hanafi responses. The first objection questions: if decreasing part of the price is valid, why is decreasing the entire price not valid by analogy? The Hanafi answer distinguishes between a change in description and a change in the essence of the contract. Decreasing the entire price effectively annuls the sale or turns it into a gift, which the parties did not intend. Therefore, it cannot be annexed. Decreasing part of the price leaves the contract intact, only changing its profit/loss description, so it is annexable. The second objection comes from Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i. They argue that annexing an increase to the original contract would result in the buyer exchanging his own property for his own property, which is invalid. The Hanafi response clarifies that annexation retroactively makes the increased price part of the original price. Thus, the exchange is between the seller's object and the buyer's total price (original plus increase), not between the buyer's property and itself. This is perfectly valid.

Key Terms
الحاق (ilhaq): Annexation of a post-contract adjustment to the original contract, treating it as if it were part of the original terms.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here the objector uses qiyas from partial to full decrease.
هبہ (hiba): Gift: a gratuitous transfer of ownership. The text considers full decrease as possibly becoming a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is decreasing the entire price after a sale contract not valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because it would require a new contract
B) Because it would either make the sale void or turn it into a gift, which the parties did not intend
C) Because the buyer must pay at least some price
D) Because the seller cannot unilaterally reduce the price

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a partial post-sale price change is valid because it is a change of description, but a full decrease changes the contract's essence and is therefore invalid; an increase is valid through annexation (ilhaq).

Annexation of Price Changes in Murabahah, Tawliyah, and Shuf'ah, and the Case of Destruction
Practical application of annexation in three scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of annexation (luhuq) of price changes to murabahah, tawliyah, and shuf'ah, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding price changes after the object is destroyed.

Core Idea
Price changes annex to the contract for resale, but not against a pre-emptor's fixed right; after destruction, only decrease is valid.

This passage from the Hidayah demonstrates how the principle of annexation (luhuq) operates in three distinct scenarios: resale at profit or cost (murabahah/tawliyah), pre-emption (shuf'ah), and when the object of sale is destroyed. Understanding these applications clarifies how post-contract adjustments affect different parties' rights, and reveals a key intra-Hanafi dispute about the legal status of a destroyed object. Let us walk through each case methodically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the annexation rule (luhuq)
The author states that a post-contract increase or decrease in price is annexed (lahaqa) to the original contract. This means the adjusted price becomes the new basis for subsequent dealings. First, understand the general rule: any change in price is treated as part of the original sale for certain purposes.
This rule applies only when the object still exists.
2
Apply to murabahah and tawliyah
If the buyer adds to the price after purchase, the new total (original + increase) becomes the cost basis for a resale on murabahah or tawliyah. Similarly, if the seller reduces the price, the reduced amount is the basis. Work through the example: buying a slave for 100, then adding 10 → resale at 110; if seller reduces by 10 → resale at 90.
This is straightforward because the same parties are involved.
3
Apply to shuf'ah and note the exception
For pre-emption, the rule differs. The pre-emptor's right attaches at the moment of the original sale. If the buyer later increases the price (e.g., adds 100 to a 1000 rupee house), the pre-emptor need only pay the original 1000, not 1100. Why? Because the seller and buyer cannot unilaterally affect a third party's established right. The increase is annexed between the original parties but not against the pre-emptor.
This is a key exception: annexation does not bind someone whose right arose before the change.
4
Analyze the destruction case – Zahir al-Riwayah
If the object is destroyed after sale but before price adjustment, the general rule changes. According to Zahir al-Riwayah (the core transmitted corpus): increasing the price is invalid because a price must be for an existing thing; you cannot pay for a non-existent object. However, decreasing the price is valid because it is an annulment (isqat) of a part of the claim, which does not require the object to exist.
The key distinction: increase needs an existing counterpart; decrease (isqat) does not.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
A riwaya from Hasan ibn Ziyad, attributed to Abu Hanifa, holds that even after destruction, the object is considered existent by estimation (taqdir), so an increase is also valid. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad follow the Zahir al-Riwayah (increase invalid). The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is the Zahir al-Riwayah, as it is more widely transmitted and aligns with the general principle that consideration requires existence.
This dispute shows how usul principles (taqdir) can lead to different rulings within the same school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ کمی اور زیادتی کا اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق ہونے کا حکم بیع مرابحہ اور بیع تولیہ میں ظاہر ہوگا۔ چنانچہ ثمن میں اضافہ کرنے کی صورت میں مرابحہ اور تولیہ اصل شمن اور اضافہ ثمن دونوں پر واقع ہوگا۔ مثلا ایک شخص نے ایک غلام ایک سور و پیہ میں خریدا پھر مشتری نے دس روپیہ کا اضافہ کیا تو مشتری اگر اس غلام کو مرا بینڈ یا تولیہ فروخت کرنا چاہے تو وہ ایک سو دس روپیہ پر مرابحہ یا تولیہ کرے گا اور اگر بائع نے ایک سور و پیشن میں سے دس روپیہ کم کر دیئے تو مشتری نوے روپیہ پر مرابحہ یا تولیہ کر سکتا ہے اور لاحق کرنے کا حکم شفعہ کی صورت میں بھی ظاہر ہو گا۔ چنانچہ اگر کسی نے ایک مکان ایک ہزار روپیہ میں خریدا پھر بائع نے ایک سوروپے کم کر دیئے تو شفیع اگر اس مکان کو لینا چا ہے تو مابقی شمن یعنی نو سو روپے کے عوض لے لے۔
وانما كان للشفيع ان ياخذ بدون الزیادۃ سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ اگر زیادتی اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق ہو جاتی ہے تو زیادتی کی صورت میں شفیع کو اصل ثمن اور اضافہ شمن دونوں کے عوض لینا چاہیے۔ مثلاً مکان ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض خریدا پھر مشتری نے ایک سو روپیہ کا اصافہ کر دیا تو اب اگر شفیع اس مکان کو مشتری سے لینا چاہے تو گیارہ سو روپیہ کے عوض لے حالانکہ ایسا نہیں ہے بلکہ حکم یہ ہے کہ شفیع اس مکان کو ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض لیگا اور زیادتی اس پر واجب نہ ہوگی۔
جواب ... اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ شفیع کا حق عقد اول یعنی ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض کے ساتھ متعلق ہو چکا ہے اور مقدار زائد یعنی ایک سو رو پیر زائد اس پر واجب کرنے میں اس کے ثابت شدہ حق کو باطل کرنا لازم آتا ہے درانحالیکہ اس میں شفیع کا نقصان بھی ہے اور بائع اور مشتری کو کسی غیر کے حق کو باطل کرنے کا قطعاً اختیار نہیں ہے اس لیے ایک سو روپیہ کی مقدار زائد بائع اور مشتری کے حق میں تو اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق ہو جائے گی ۔ لیکن شفیع کے حق میں لاحق نہ ہوگی۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ تمام تفاصیل اس وقت ہیں جبکہ مبیع موجود ہو لیکن اگر مبیع ہلاک ہوگئی تو ظاہر الروایہ کے حکم کے مطابق شمن کے اندر زیادتی کرنا صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ ہلاک ہونے کے بعد مبیع ایسی حالت پر باقی نہیں رہی جس کا عوض لینا درست ہو ۔ اس لئے که عوض موجود چیز کا ہوتا ہے معدوم چیز کا عوض نہیں ہوتا اور قاعدہ ہے کہ شئی پہلے خود ثابت ہوتی ہے پھر کسی کی طرف منسوب ہوتی ہے حالانکہ اس جگہ میچ موجود نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے زیادتی شمن ہی ثابت نہیں ہے تو یہ زیادتی اصل عقد کی طرف منسوب ہو کر اس کے ساتھ لاحق بھی نہ ہوگی۔ ہاں صبیح ہلاک ہونے کے بعد اگر شمن میں کمی کی گئی تو اس کمی کو اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ یمن کم کرنا استقاط ( ساقط کرنا) ہے اور ساقط کرنے کے لیے اس کے مقابل کا موجود ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے اس لئے کم کرنا فی الحال ثابت ہو کر اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق ہو جائے گا حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ سے حسن بن زیاد کی روایت میں ہے کہ میچ اگر چہ حقیقتا بلاک ہو گئی ہے لیکن اس کو تقدیر موجود قرار دیا جائے گا۔
The author of Hidayah says: The ruling that decrease and increase are annexed to the original contract will become evident in murabahah and tawliyah sales. Thus, in the case of an increase in price, murabahah and tawliyah will be based on the original price and the increase together. For example, a person bought a slave for one hundred rupees, then the buyer added ten rupees. If the buyer wants to sell this slave on murabahah or tawliyah, he will do so at one hundred and ten rupees. And if the seller decreased ten rupees from the hundred, the buyer can do murabahah or tawliyah at ninety rupees. And the ruling of annexation also appears in the case of shuf'ah (pre-emption). For example, if someone bought a house for one thousand rupees, then the seller decreased one hundred rupees, then the pre-emptor (shafi') can take this house for the remaining price, i.e., nine hundred rupees. And the phrase 'the pre-emptor has the right to take without the increase' answers a question. The question is: If the increase is annexed to the original contract, then in the case of an increase, the pre-emptor should take against both the original price and the increase. For example, a house was bought for one thousand rupees, then the buyer added one hundred rupees. So if the pre-emptor wants to take this house from the buyer, he should take it for eleven hundred rupees, but this is not the case. Rather, the ruling is that the pre-emptor will take the house for one thousand rupees, and the increase will not be obligatory upon him. Answer: The answer is that the pre-emptor's right is attached to the first contract, i.e., the one thousand rupees, and making the excess amount (one hundred rupees) obligatory upon him would annul his established right, especially since there is a loss for the pre-emptor, and the seller and buyer have absolutely no authority to annul another's right. Therefore, the excess amount of one hundred rupees will be annexed to the original contract with respect to the seller and buyer, but not with respect to the pre-emptor. The author of Hidayah says: All these details apply when the object (mabi') is extant. But if the object is destroyed, then according to the ruling of Zahir al-Riwayah, increasing the price is not valid, because after destruction the object remains in a state where it is not correct to take a price for it. Because a price is for an existing thing, not for a non-existing thing. And the rule is that a thing is first established itself, then it is attributed to something else. Here, since the object does not exist, the increase in price is not itself established, so this increase cannot be attributed to the original contract and annexed. Yes, if the price is decreased after the object is destroyed, this decrease is annexed to the original contract. Because decreasing the price is an annulment (isqat), and for annulment, the existence of the counterpart is not necessary. Therefore, the decrease becomes established immediately and is annexed to the original contract. In the riwaya of Hasan ibn Ziyad from Imam Abu Hanifa, it is that even if the object is literally destroyed, it is considered as existent in estimation (taqdir).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage applies the principle of annexation to three practical scenarios: murabahah/tawliyah (resale at profit/cost), shuf'ah (pre-emption), and the case of destruction of the object. In murabahah/tawliyah, any post-contract increase or decrease in price is included in the cost basis for the resale. In shuf'ah, the pre-emptor's right is established based on the original price at the time of the sale; a subsequent increase by the buyer does not bind the pre-emptor because his right accrued before the increase. The seller and buyer cannot affect a third party's established right. Finally, the passage discusses the situation when the object (mabi') is destroyed. According to the Zahir al-Riwayah (the transmitted corpus of Imam Muhammad), increasing the price after destruction is invalid because there is no existing object to serve as a countervalue. However, decreasing the price is valid because it amounts to a remission (isqat) which does not require the counterpart to exist. A different riwaya from Hasan ibn Ziyad, attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa, holds that the destroyed object is considered existent by estimation (taqdir), thus allowing an increase as well. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a stated profit.
تولية (tawliyah): A sale at exactly the purchase price (cost), without profit or loss.
شفعة (shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property sold to a third party at the same price.
اسقاط (isqat): Annulment or remission of a right or liability. Here, decreasing the price is considered an isqat of a part of the claim.
تقدير (taqdir): Legal estimation; treating a thing as existent even if physically destroyed, for legal purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah, is it valid to increase the price after the object of sale is destroyed?
A) Yes, because the parties can agree to any change
B) No, because the object no longer exists to serve as a countervalue
C) No, but only if the destruction was intentional
D) Yes, by considering the object as existent in estimation

Key Takeaway
Price changes annex to the original contract for resale (murabahah/tawliyah) and bind the original parties, but a pre-emptor's right is protected from post-sale increases; after destruction, only a decrease (isqat) is valid according to the relied-upon position.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Attachment of Increase or Decrease to Original Contract in Murabahah, Tawliyah, and Shuf'ah
Explains the ruling on price adjustments attaching to original contracts and their application to preemption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on how post-contract price adjustments attach to the original contract in murabahah, tawliyah, and shuf'ah, and distinguish the treatment of preemption rights.

Core Idea
A price increase after contract attaches for murabahah/tawliyah but not against the preemptor; a decrease always attaches, even after property destruction.

In Hanafi commercial law, when a buyer or seller later adjusts the price of a sold item, the adjustment may or may not attach to the original contract depending on the type of sale and the presence of third-party rights like preemption. This lesson explains the rule with examples and the pivotal distinction between increase and decrease, especially when the sold property perishes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic principle
When the price is increased or decreased post-contract, for the purpose of murabahah and tawliyah, it is as if the new total is the original price. For preemption, only the original price binds.
2
Apply the rule to murabahah/tawliyah
If seller reduces price, buyer resells at reduced cost; if buyer increases price, resells at increased cost. Both are valid.
3
Analyze the preemption exception
The preemptor's right is fixed at the first contract price. A later increase cannot be forced on him because that would harm his established right. Seller and buyer cannot revoke his right.
4
Consider the case of property destruction
According to Zahir al-Riwayah, increase after destruction is invalid because you cannot attach an increase to a non-existent object. Decrease is valid because it is a cancellation (isqat) that does not require the object's existence. Note the minority view of Abu Hanifa.

دس روپیہ جب اصل عقد کے ساتھ مل گئے تو ایسا ہو گیا گویا مشترکی نے ابتداء صبیح[؟] ایک سو دس روپیہ کے عوض خریدی ہے اور جب مشترکی نے ابتداء مبیع ایک سو دس روپیہ کے عوض خریدی تو مشتری کی ملک کا مشتری کی ملک کے عوض ہونا لازم نہیں آئے گا بلکہ مائع[؟] کی ملک یعنی مبیع مشترکی کی ملک یعنی ایک سو دس روپیہ کے عوض ہو جائے گی اور اس میں کوئی قباحت نہیں ہے۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ کمی اور زیادتی کا اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق ہونے کا حکم بیع مرابحہ اور بیع تولیہ میں ظاہر ہوگا۔ چنانچہ ثمن میں اضافہ کرنے کی صورت میں مرابحہ اور تولیہ اصل شمن اور اضافہ ثمن دونوں پر واقع ہوگا۔ مثلا ایک شخص نے ایک غلام ایک سور و پیہ میں خریدا پھر مشتری نے دس روپیہ کا اضافہ کیا تو مشتری اگر اس غلام کو مرا بینڈ یا تولیہ فروخت کرنا چاہے تو وہ ایک سو دس روپیہ پر مرابحہ یا تولیہ کرے گا اور اگر بائع نے ایک سور و پیشن میں سے دس روپیہ کم کر دیئے تو مشتری نوے روپیہ پر مرابحہ یا تولیہ کر سکتا ہے اور لاحق کرنے کا حکم شفعہ کی صورت میں بھی ظاہر ہو گا۔ چنانچہ اگر کسی نے ایک مکان ایک ہزار روپیہ میں خریدا پھر بائع نے ایک سوروپے کم کر دیئے تو شفیع اگر اس مکان کو لینا چا ہے تو مابقی شمن یعنی نو سو روپے کے عوض لے لے۔
وانما كان للشفيع ان ياخذ بدون الزیادۃ سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ اگر زیادتی اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق ہو جاتی ہے تو زیادتی کی صورت میں شفیع کو اصل ثمن اور اضافہ شمن دونوں کے عوض لینا چاہیے۔ مثلاً مکان ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض خریدا پھر مشتری نے ایک سو روپیہ کا اصافہ کر دیا تو اب اگر شفیع اس مکان کو مشتری سے لینا چاہے تو گیارہ سو روپیہ کے عوض لے حالانکہ ایسا نہیں ہے بلکہ حکم یہ ہے کہ شفیع اس مکان کو ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض لیگا اور زیادتی اس پر واجب نہ ہوگی۔
جواب ... اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ شفیع کا حق عقد اول یعنی ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض کے ساتھ متعلق ہو چکا ہے اور مقدار زائد یعنی ایک سو رو پیر زائد اس پر واجب کرنے میں اس کے ثابت شدہ حق کو باطل کرنا لازم آتا ہے درانحالیکہ اس میں شفیع کا نقصان بھی ہے اور بائع اور مشتری کو کسی غیر کے حق کو باطل کرنے کا قطعاً اختیار نہیں ہے اس لیے ایک سو روپیہ کی مقدار زائد بائع اور مشتری کے حق میں تو اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق ہو جائے گی ۔ لیکن شفیع کے حق میں لاحق نہ ہوگی۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ تمام تفاصیل اس وقت ہیں جبکہ مبیع موجود ہو لیکن اگر مبیع ہلاک ہوگئی تو ظاہر الروایہ کے حکم کے مطابق شمن کے اندر زیادتی کرنا صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ ہلاک ہونے کے بعد مبیع ایسی حالت پر باقی نہیں رہی جس کا عوض لینا درست ہو ۔ اس لئے که عوض موجود چیز کا ہوتا ہے معدوم چیز کا عوض نہیں ہوتا اور قاعدہ ہے کہ شئی پہلے خود ثابت ہوتی ہے پھر کسی کی طرف منسوب ہوتی ہے حالانکہ اس جگہ میچ موجود نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے زیادتی شمن ہی ثابت نہیں ہے تو یہ زیادتی اصل عقد کی طرف منسوب ہو کر اس کے ساتھ لاحق بھی نہ ہوگی۔ ہاں صبیح ہلاک ہونے کے بعد اگر شمن میں کمی کی گئی تو اس کمی کو اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ یمن کم کرنا استقاط ( ساقط کرنا) ہے اور ساقط کرنے کے لیے اس کے مقابل کا موجود ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے اس لئے کم کرنا فی الحال ثابت ہو کر اصل عقد کے ساتھ لاحق ہو جائے گا حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ سے حسن بن زیاد کی روایت میں ہے کہ میچ اگر چہ حقیقتا بلاک ہو گئی ہے لیکن اس کو تقدیر موجود قرار دیا جائے گا۔
When ten rupees are added to the original contract, it becomes as if the buyer initially purchased the commodity for one hundred and ten rupees. And when the buyer initially purchased the commodity for one hundred and ten rupees, it is not necessary that the buyer's ownership be in exchange for the buyer's ownership; rather, the seller's ownership, i.e., the sold property, is in exchange for the buyer's ownership, i.e., one hundred and ten rupees, and there is no problem in this. The author of Al-Hidayah says that the ruling of increase and decrease attaching to the original contract becomes apparent in the sale of murabahah and tawliyah. Thus, in the case of an increase in price, murabahah and tawliyah will be based on both the original price and the increase. For example, a person bought a slave for one hundred rupees, then the buyer added ten rupees; if the buyer wants to sell this slave as murabahah or tawliyah, he will do so on one hundred and ten rupees. And if the seller reduced ten rupees from the one hundred, then the buyer can do murabahah or tawliyah on ninety rupees. And the ruling of attachment also becomes apparent in the case of preemption (shuf'ah). For instance, if someone bought a house for one thousand rupees, then the seller reduced one hundred rupees, then the preemptor, if he wants to take this house, he takes it in exchange for the remaining price, i.e., nine hundred rupees. The statement 'And the preemptor has the right to take without the increase' is an answer to a question. The question is: if the increase attaches to the original contract, then in the case of an increase, the preemptor should take the house in exchange for both the original price and the increase. For example, the house was bought for one thousand rupees, then the buyer added one hundred rupees; now if the preemptor wants to take this house from the buyer, he should take it for one thousand one hundred rupees, but this is not the case; rather, the ruling is that the preemptor will take this house for one thousand rupees, and the increase is not obligatory upon him. The answer is that the right of the preemptor is already connected with the first contract, i.e., the exchange of one thousand rupees, and making the additional amount, i.e., one hundred rupees, obligatory upon him entails invalidating his established right, while this also involves harm to the preemptor. The seller and buyer have absolutely no authority to invalidate the right of another. Therefore, the additional amount of one hundred rupees attaches to the original contract with respect to the seller and buyer, but does not attach with respect to the preemptor. The author of Al-Hidayah says that all these details apply when the sold property exists. However, if the sold property perishes, then according to the ruling of Zahir al-Riwayah, increasing the price is not valid because after perishing, the sold property no longer remains in a state that can validly be exchanged. This is because exchange is for an existing thing, not for a non-existent thing. And the rule is that a thing first itself is established, then it is attributed to someone. But here, due to the non-existence of the property, the increase in price itself is not established, so this increase will not be attached to the original contract. Yes, if a reduction in price is made after the property perishes, then this reduction will be attached to the original contract because reducing the price is a cancellation, and for cancellation, the existence of its counterpart is not necessary. Therefore, the reduction is immediately established and attaches to the original contract. In the narration of Hasan ibn Ziyad from Imam Abu Hanifah, it is stated that even if the property has perished in reality, it will be considered as if it were existing.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining how increases or decreases in price attach to the original contract in Hanafi law, specifically in murabahah, tawliyah, and shuf'ah. The author, commenting on Al-Hidayah, begins by illustrating the principle: when the buyer later adds to the price, it is treated as if the original price was the increased amount for future transactions. The same logic applies to decreases. Two applications are given: first, in murabahah (sale at cost plus profit) and tawliyah (sale at cost), where the price includes any subsequent adjustment; second, in shuf'ah (preemption), where the preemptor's right is based on the original price only, not any later increase. The key distinction is that the preemptor's right is established at the time of the first contract, and a subsequent increase cannot be forced on him because that would harm his existing right. The seller and buyer cannot unilaterally affect the preemptor's rights. The passage then addresses the situation when the sold property is destroyed. According to the Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books of Imam Muhammad), increasing the price after destruction is invalid because you cannot attach an increase to a non-existent object. However, a reduction is valid because it is a cancellation (isqat), which does not require the existence of the object. A minority narration from Imam Abu Hanifa through Hasan ibn Ziyad considers the destroyed property as existent in estimate, allowing increase. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the Zahir al-Riwayah view.

Key Terms
مرابحہ (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit margin; the buyer knows the cost.
تولیہ (tawliyah): A sale at cost price, without profit; the buyer pays exactly what the seller paid.
شفعہ (shuf'ah): Preemption right of a co-owner or neighbor to buy the property at the price paid by the outsider.
استقاط (isqat): Cancellation or waiver of a right; in this context, reducing the price is considered an isqat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the sold property is destroyed, what is the ruling on a subsequent increase in price according to Zahir al-Riwayah?
A) The increase is valid and attaches to the original contract.
B) The increase is invalid because the sold property no longer exists.
C) The increase is valid only if the buyer agrees.
D) The increase is considered a new contract.

Key Takeaway
The rule on attachment of price adjustments in Hanafi law respects both the contractual integration for future sales and the fixed rights of third parties like preemptors.

Deferring the Price After a Cash Sale: Known Terms, Unknown Terms, and the Hanafi Position
Ruling on deferring the price after a cash sale, with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on deferring the price after a cash sale, apply the distinction between known and unknown terms, and evaluate the opposing argument of Zufar and Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A seller may defer his own right; a known term makes the price deferred; slight ignorance in the term is tolerated, excessive is not.

This lesson tackles a common practical scenario: after agreeing on a cash sale, the seller gives the buyer extra time to pay. Is this permissible? Does it change the nature of the debt? The Hanafi school, along with Imam Malik, says yes—the price becomes deferred and the seller cannot demand payment before the term. But Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i disagree, arguing that a debt is like a loan and cannot be deferred by a subsequent agreement. The passage also distinguishes between a known term (valid), an excessively unknown term (invalid), and a slightly unknown term (valid by analogy with kafalah). Let's unpack the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling and its basis
Read the matn: if a seller sets a known term after a cash sale, the price becomes deferred. The key principle: the price is the seller's right; he can waive it entirely, so he can certainly waive demand for a time.
This is the core Hanafi position.
2
Understand the opposing analogy (loan vs. proprietary right)
Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i analogize the price to a loan (qard): a loan does not become deferred by a term, so the price remains due immediately. The Hanafi response distinguishes: the seller has proprietary authority over his own right, unlike a lender whose right is a debt owed by another.
The qiyas is rejected because the subject matter differs.
3
Distinguish between jahalah fahishah and jahalah yasirah
When the term is unknown, two subcases: excessive ignorance (e.g., 'when the wind blows') invalidates the deferment; slight ignorance (e.g., 'at harvest time') is valid, by analogy with kafalah where slight ignorance in the term is forgiven.
This is a practical distinction: 'harvest' is near to knowledge, 'wind' is indefinite.
4
Note the exception: term in the original contract
The passage ends with a warning: if the term is a condition in the original sale contract, it renders the contract fasid regardless of the degree of ignorance. This was detailed in previous lessons.
Do not confuse post-sale deferment with in-contract term.

تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی آدمی نے کوئی چیز نقد شمن کے عوض فروخت کی پھر بائع نے ثمن ادا کرنے کے لئے ایک میعاد مقرر کی تو اس کی دو صورتیں ہیں۔ میعاد معلوم ہوگی یا مجہول ہوگی۔ اگر میعاد معلوم ہے معلوم مقرر کرنے کے باوجود بائع فی الحال شمن کا مطالبہ کر سکتا ہے۔ امام زفر اور امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ شمن مشتری کے ذمہ میں دین ہوتا ہے اور دین قرض پر قیاس کیا گیا ہے یعنی جس طرح قرض میعاد مقرر
