کیساتھ فاسد ہو جاتی ہے تفصیل سابق میں گذر چکی ہے۔
He said: And whoever sells for a cash price, then defers it to a known term, it becomes deferred because the price is his right, and he has the right to postpone it to ease the one who owes it. Do you not see that he has the right to absolve him completely, so likewise for a limited time. If he defers it to an unknown term, if the ignorance is excessive, like the blowing of the wind, it is not permissible. If it is near, like harvesting and threshing, it is permissible because it is like a guarantee, and we have mentioned it before. Translation: And if someone sells something for a cash price, then (for the buyer) he sets a known term, then this price becomes deferred. Because the price is the right of the seller, so he has the authority to defer it for the ease of the one on whom it is due (the buyer). Do you not see that the seller has the authority to absolve the buyer completely, so likewise he will have the authority to defer for a limited time. And if he sets an unknown term for him, if the ignorance is excessive, like the blowing of the wind, it is not permissible. If the ignorance is near to understanding, like harvesting and threshing, it is permissible because it is like a guarantee, and we have mentioned it before. Explanation: The case is that if a person sells something for a cash price, then the seller sets a term for payment, there are two possibilities. The term may be known or unknown. If the term is known, then setting this term is permissible, and this is the saying of Imam Malik. Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i said that setting a term does not make the price deferred, even if the term is known. According to these two, despite setting a known term, the seller can demand the price immediately. Their proof is that the price is a debt in the buyer's liability, and debt is analogized to a loan, just as a loan does not become deferred even with a term, so too debt will not become deferred. Our proof is that the price is the seller's right, and every owner of a right has authority to dispose of his right. Therefore, the seller has the authority to postpone the demand for his right until a known period to facilitate the buyer. A second proof is that the seller setting a term for payment is absolving the buyer from demand until the known term, and since the seller has the authority to absolve the buyer completely from the price, then a fortiori he has the authority to absolve from demand for a limited time. And if the term is unknown, there are also two possibilities: excessive ignorance or slight ignorance. If the ignorance is excessive, e.g., setting the term until the wind blows or rain falls, it is not permissible, meaning the term is not considered and the seller can demand at any time. If the ignorance is slight, e.g., until the harvest is cut or the grain is threshed, it is permissible, meaning the seller cannot demand before the term ends. The proof is that deferring the price after the contract is like a guarantee, and it has passed before that a guarantee with slight ignorance is valid, but with excessive ignorance it is not. Similarly, deferring the debt price with slight ignorance is valid, but with excessive ignorance it is not. And if the term is made a condition in the contract of sale itself, then the contract becomes invalid with both excessive and slight ignorance; details have passed before.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding whether a seller may defer the price after a cash sale. The matn from Al-Hidayah states that if the seller sets a known term after concluding a cash sale, the price becomes deferred. This is the Hanafi position, supported by Imam Malik. The Hanafi proof is that the price is the seller's right, and he may waive it completely or partially; thus he may postpone the demand. Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i argue that a debt is like a loan (qard), which does not become deferred by a subsequent term, so the seller can still demand immediate payment. The Hanafis respond with a stronger analogy: the seller's authority over his own right is absolute, and deferral is a form of partial waiver (waiver of demand), which is a fortiori permissible given the permission of full waiver. The passage then discusses unknown terms. A term with excessive ignorance (e.g., 'when the wind blows') is invalid, and the price remains due immediately. A term with slight ignorance (e.g., 'at harvest time') is valid, by analogy with guarantee (kafalah), where slight ignorance in the term is acceptable. An important distinction is made: if the term is a condition in the original contract, it invalidates the contract regardless of the degree of ignorance. This lesson underscores the Hanafi principle that a seller may dispose of his right as he wishes, and that analogy with loan is rejected in favor of direct authority over one's own right.

Key Terms
ثمن حال (thaman haal): Spot price or cash price due immediately at the time of sale.
اجل معلوم (ajal ma'loom): A known, fixed term or period for payment, such as a specific date or month.
جہالت فاحشہ (jahalah fahishah): Excessive ignorance or uncertainty about the term, e.g., 'when the wind blows' – renders the deferment invalid.
جہالت متقاربة / یسیرہ (jahalah mutaqaribah / yasirah): Slight ignorance that is near to understanding, e.g., 'at harvest time' – considered valid in deferment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a seller sets a term of 'until the wind blows' after a cash sale?
A) The deferment is valid and the seller cannot demand payment until the wind blows.
B) The deferment is invalid because the term is excessively unknown; the seller may demand payment immediately.
C) The sale becomes invalid because the term is a condition in the contract.
D) The seller must set a known term within one month.

Key Takeaway
A seller may validly defer his price after a cash sale to a known term or a term with slight ignorance; excessive ignorance invalidates the deferment but not the original sale.

Postponing a Cash Sale Price: The Ruling and Its Proof
Establishes that a seller may defer a cash sale price with a known term, countering objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on deferring a cash sale price with a known term, explain the evidence based on ibra' and qiyas, and distinguish the position from Imam Zufar and Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A seller may defer a cash price for a known term because his right includes partial waiver, not just total remission.

When a seller sells for cash but later agrees to a deferred payment period, does the sale become deferred? The Hanafi school says yes, provided the term is known. This lesson unpacks the ruling, examines the proofs from the seller's absolute right to remit, and addresses the objections raised by Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i who analogize such a deferred price to a loan. You will learn the legal reasoning behind the Hanafi position and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
From the passage, extract the core ruling: if a seller sells for cash then sets a known term, the sale becomes deferred. Also note the treatment of unknown terms: excessive ignorance (like wind) invalidates, but minor ignorance (like harvest) is valid.
The ruling is about post-sale deferment, not stipulation in the contract.
2
Understand the primary proof: the seller's right
The passage argues that the price is the seller's right. He can completely remit it (ibra'), so he can also partially postpone it. This is a strong rational proof (qiyas jaliy) based on the analogy of complete and partial waiver.
The proof relies on the principle that the greater right includes the lesser.
3
Analyze the opposing analogy
Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i analogize the deferred price to a loan (qard), where deferment is invalid. Identify their reasoning: dayn (debt) from sale is similar to qard, so the same rule should apply.
This is a qiyas khafi (subtle analogy) based on the generic category of debt.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The passage distinguishes qard from sale: qard is a benevolent contract (tabarru') while sale is a commutative contract (mu'awada). The seller's right to remit entirely is exclusive to sale, making the analogy to ibra' stronger.
The Hanafi response uses istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas.
5
Apply to unknown terms
Note the two categories of unknown terms: excessive ignorance (like wind) makes deferment invalid, minor ignorance (like harvest) makes it valid. This parallels the rules of kafala (suretyship) as the passage mentions.
The justification is that minor ignorance is tolerated to avoid hardship, while excessive ignorance causes gharar.

میعاد مقررکرنا ہمشتری کو میعاد معلوم کے آنے تک شمن کے مطالبہ سے بری کرنا ہے اور بائع کو چونکہ یہ اختیار حاصل ہے کہ وہ شمن ہی سے مشتری کو بری کر دے تو ایک وقت محدود تک مطالبہ سے بری کرنے کا اختیار بدرجہ اولی حاصل ہوگا اور اگر میعاد مجہول ہو تو اس کی بھی دو صورتیں ہیں جہالت فاحشہ ہوگی یا جہالت سیرہ ہوگی اگر جہالت فاحشہ ہے مثلاً ادائے شمن کے لیے ہوا چلنے یا بارش برسنے تک کی میعاد مقرر کی تو یہ جائز نہیں ہے یعنی اس میعاد کا اعتبار نہ ہوگا بلکہ بائع کو ہمہ وقت مطالبہ کا اختیار حاصل ہوگا اور اگر جہالت بیرہ ہو مثلاً ادائے شمن کے لیے کھیتی کٹنے یا گا بنے تک کی میعاد مقرر کی گئی تو یہ جائز ہے یعنی اس میعاد کے پورا ہونے سے پہلے بائع کو مطالبہ کا حق نہ ہو گا دلیل یہ ہے کہ عقد بیع کے بعد ثمن کو مؤجل کرنا بمنزلہ کفالہ کے ہے۔ اور سابق میں گذر چکا ب کہ کفالہ جہالت بیرہ کیسا تھ تو صحیح ہو جاتا ہے لیکن جہالت فاحشہ کے ساتھ صحیح نہیں ہوتا پس اسی طرح دین شمن کو جہالت بیرہ کے ساتھ موؤ جل کرنا تو درست ہے لیکن جہالت فاحشہ کے ساتھ موجعل کرنا درست نہیں ہے اور اگر عقد بیع میں اجل کو شرط قراردیدیا گیا تو عقد ی جہالت فاحشہ اور جہالت بیرہ دونوں کیساتھ فاسد ہو جاتی ہے تفصیل سابق میں گذر چکی ہے۔
He said: Whoever sells for an immediate price then postpones it to a known term, it becomes deferred. Because the price is his right, and he has the authority to delay it to ease the one who owes it. Do you not see that he has the authority to completely renounce it? So also [he has authority] for a specific time. If he postpones it to an unknown term: if the ignorance is excessive like the blowing of wind, it is not permissible; if it is close [to being known] like harvesting and threshing, it is permissible, because it is like a guarantee, and we mentioned it before. Translation: If someone sold something for a cash price then set a known term for the buyer, the sale becomes deferred. Because the price is the seller's right, so he has the option to defer it to ease the debtor. Do you not see that the seller can completely absolve the buyer? Similarly, he can postpone it for a limited time. If he sets an unknown term: if the ignorance is excessive like the wind blowing, it is not permissible; if the ignorance is close [to being known] like harvesting and threshing, it is permissible because it is like a guarantee, which we mentioned before. Explanation: The scenario is that if a person sold something for a cash price, then the seller set a term for payment. There are two cases: the term is known or unknown. If the term is known, setting the term is permissible, and this is the view of Imam Malik. Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i ruled that the sale does not become deferred even if the term is known. Thus, according to them, the seller can demand the price immediately despite the term. Their evidence is that the price is a debt (dayn) in the buyer's liability, and debt is analogized to loan (qard): just as a loan does not become deferred by setting a term, so too the debt does not become deferred. Our evidence is that the price is the seller's right, and every owner of a right has the authority to dispose of his right. Therefore, the seller has the right to postpone demanding his right for a known term to ease the buyer. The second evidence is that setting a term for payment is like absolving the buyer from demand until that term, and since the seller has the right to completely absolve the buyer, it is more appropriate that he has the right to absolve for a limited time. If the term is unknown, there are two cases: excessive ignorance or minor ignorance. If excessive like the wind blowing or rain, it is not valid; the term is disregarded and the seller can demand at any time. If minor like harvesting and threshing, it is valid; the seller cannot demand before that. The evidence is that deferring the price after the sale is like a guarantee, and it was mentioned before that a guarantee with minor ignorance is valid but with excessive ignorance is not. Thus, deferring the debt with minor ignorance is correct but not with excessive ignorance. If the term is stipulated in the sale contract itself, the contract becomes invalid with both types of ignorance; details were given before.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof (hukm bi dalilihi) from the Hanafi school, addressing whether a seller can retrospectively defer a cash sale price. The passage opens by stating the ruling: if a seller sells for an immediate price then later sets a known term, the sale becomes deferred. The proof is that the price is the seller's right, and like complete remission, partial deferment is permissible. The text then addresses the dispute: Imam Zufar (Hanafi dissenter) and Imam Shafi'i argue by analogy with qard (loan), where deferment is invalid. The Hanafi response distinguishes: qard is a benevolent loan (ariyah) initially, while dayn from sale is a right that can be waived entirely, so partial waiver is also valid. The passage also covers unknown terms: excessive ignorance (like wind) invalidates the deferment, while minor ignorance (like harvest) is valid, analogizing to kafala (suretyship). The Urdu commentary further clarifies the two cases and the principle that the seller's right to defer is derived from his right to completely remit. The passage emphasizes istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas in this matter.

Key Terms
ثمن حال (thaman hāl): The price that is due immediately (cash price) in a sale contract.
أجل معلوم (ajal ma'lūm): A known term or deadline set for payment.
إبراء (ibrā'): Legal remission or discharge of a debt by the creditor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a seller sells for cash then later sets a known term for payment, what is the ruling?
A) The sale remains cash; the seller can demand payment immediately.
B) The sale becomes deferred; the seller cannot demand payment before the term.
C) The sale becomes invalid because the term is a new condition.
D) The sale becomes a loan contract.

Key Takeaway
A seller may defer a cash price for a known term because his right to partial waiver is derived from his right to complete remission.

Deferring an Immediate Debt: The Exception of Qarḍ and the Wasiyya Exception
Ruling with proof on deferment of immediate debts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule on deferring an immediate debt and explain why deferring a loan (qarḍ) is invalid in the Hanafi school, including the exception for a bequest (wasiyya).

Core Idea
A creditor may defer any debt except a loan — deferment of a loan is like selling money for money on credit, which is ribā.

We have learned that a debt is the creditor's right, so they can demand it immediately or postpone it. But what about a loan (qarḍ)? The Hanafi school holds that a loan is a charitable act at its start, so you cannot bind the debtor to a later payment. However, if someone bequeaths a deferred loan, the heirs must honour it. This passage explains the reasoning and the exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and exception
Locate the statement that every immediate debt becomes deferred when the creditor sets a term, and the exception for qarḍ.
2
Understand the dual nature of qarḍ
Analyze how qarḍ is considered both a charitable act (inception) and an exchange (conclusion), and how this dual nature affects deferment.
Inception: tabarru'; conclusion: mu'awada.
3
Follow the Hanafi reasoning
Trace two lines of reasoning: (1) analogy to 'āriyah (borrowing) where deferment is not binding, and (2) if deferment were valid, it would become a sale of money for money on credit (ribā).
4
Recognize the exception for wasiyya
Note that if a person bequeaths a deferred loan, the heirs are obliged to give it and cannot demand before the term, because it is a bequest of charity.
This exception is based on the binding nature of a valid bequest.

Source Text

ادھار ہو جائے گی حالانکہ یہ ر ہوا ہے اور یہ اس صورت کے برخلاف ہے کہ جب ایک آدمی نے وصیت کی کہ اس کے مال میں سے ایک ہزار درہم فلاں کو ایک سال کے ادھار قرض دیدینا تو ورثہ پر لازم ہوگا کہ اس کے تہائی مال میں سے اس کو قرضہ دیدیں اور مدت سے پہلے اس سے مطالبہ نہ کریں۔ کیونکہ یہ تہر کرنے کی وصیت ہے جیسے غلام کی خدمت اور مکان میں رہنے کی وصیت کی ہو تو یہ موصی کے حق کی وجہ سے لا زم ہوگی ۔ تشریح قرض اور دین درست ہو جائیگا اور میعاد باطل ہو جائے گی۔ البتہ امام مالک کے نزدیک قرضہ میں میعاد مقرر کرنا، امت سے امام مالک کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ( قرنہ ( قرضدار کے ذمہ میں واجب ہوتا ہے جیسا کہ دوسرے ڈیون واجب ہوتے ہیں جگہ
He said: Every immediate debt, when its owner defers it, becomes deferred, due to what we mentioned, except the loan (qarḍ), for its deferment is not valid, because it is a loan and a gratuitous act in its inception, to the extent that it is valid with the wording of borrowing, and the one who does not own charity like the executor and the minor cannot do it, and it is an exchange at its conclusion. So considering the inception, deferment is not binding in it, as in borrowing, because there is no compulsion in charity. And considering the conclusion, it is not valid because it becomes a sale of dirhams with dirhams on credit, which is ribā. This is contrary to when one bequeaths that one thousand dirhams be given as a loan for one year: in that case the heirs are obliged to give it as a loan from one third of the estate, and they cannot demand it before the term, because it is a bequest of charity, like a bequest of service or dwelling; it becomes a right for the testator. Translation: Every immediate debt, when the creditor sets a term for the debtor, it becomes deferred. The proof was given earlier: the debt is the creditor's right; he can completely drop it or postpone it for the debtor's ease. However, in our school, if a term is set for a loan (qarḍ), it is not valid; after setting the term, the creditor can demand immediate payment. In short, the loan is valid but the term is void. Imam Malik holds that setting a term in a loan is valid, arguing that a loan is a debt like other debts, so deferment is valid. Our evidence is that a loan is initially a gift and charity (tabarru'), so it is like borrowing (ariyah). By analogy, just as in borrowing deferment is not valid, so too in loan. Also, considering the conclusion, it would become a sale of money for money on credit, which is ribā. Exception: If a person bequeaths that one thousand dirhams be given as a loan to someone for one year, the heirs are obliged from one third of the estate to give that loan and cannot demand it before the term, because it is a bequest of charity, like a bequest of service or dwelling; it becomes a right for the testator. Explanation: The difference between qarḍ and dayn: qarḍ is the specific property given for benefit; what becomes due in the debtor's liability is dayn, not qarḍ. Dayn is anything obligatory in the liability, whether from a contract or tort. The ruling is that every immediate dayn becomes deferred when the creditor sets a known term, except qarḍ. The proof: the debt is the creditor's right, and he can drop it completely or postpone it. But for qarḍ, it is invalid because initially it is a gift, and deferment would be like invalidating the gift temporarily. Imam Malik's view is countered by the reasoning that qarḍ is considered as a voluntary act that cannot be subject to a binding deferment.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents a general principle and an exception. It begins by stating that any immediate debt (dayn hāl) becomes deferred when the creditor sets a term, citing the previous reasoning that the debt is the creditor's right. The exception is qarḍ (loan). The text explains why deferment of a qarḍ is invalid: because a qarḍ is initially an 'āriyah (loan for use) and a tabarru' (gratuitous act); thus, it is not binding to defer it, as in borrowing. Moreover, if deferment were valid, it would become a sale of money on credit, constituting ribā. The text then provides an exception to the exception: a wasiyya (bequest) directing that a qarḍ be given with a term is binding on the heirs from one third of the estate, because it is a bequest of charity. The Urdu commentary elaborates the distinction between qarḍ and dayn, and notes that Imam Malik disagrees on the validity of deferring qarḍ. The Hanafi position is based on the nature of qarḍ as a gratuitous act; the analogy to 'āriyah is key. The passage also includes the important point that setting a term in a qarḍ does not nullify the loan; the loan is valid but the term is void.

Key Terms
دين حال (dayn hāl): A debt that is immediately due and payable.
قرض (qarḍ): A loan of fungible property given by one person to another for benefit, creating a debt (dayn) in the liability.
عارية (ʿāriyah): A loan for use, where the lender allows use of an object without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if someone gives a loan (qarḍ) and sets a term for repayment?
A) The loan becomes invalid.
B) The term is valid and the borrower cannot be demanded before the term.
C) The loan is valid but the term is void; the lender can demand repayment immediately.
D) The lender cannot demand repayment at all because the term is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a loan cannot be deferred because it begins as a gift; but a bequest to give a deferred loan is binding on the heirs.

The Ruling on Deferring Loans (Qard) in Hanafi Fiqh
Explains that loans cannot be deferred, contrasting with other debts and refuting Imam Malik

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a loan (qard) cannot be deferred in Hanafi fiqh, explain why it differs from other debts using its dual nature, and distinguish the exception for bequests.

Core Idea
A loan is a donation at inception; a deferment term would coerce a donation or create riba.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a critical distinction: while most debts become deferred when a term is set, loans (qard) are an exception. The ruling hinges on the dual nature of qard as both donation and compensation. Understanding this helps avoid errors in transactional practice and clarifies the Madhhab's method of usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and exception
The text states that any debt that is due becomes deferred if the owner sets a term, except for qard. Identify that qard is the exception.
2
Analyze the dual nature of qard
The author explains that qard is a donation (like 'ariyah) at the start and a compensation at the end. Understand why this matters.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof uses qiyas khafi (subtle analogy) to distinguish qard from other debts. Note that because it is a donation at the start, deferment would be like coercing a donation, and at the end it would be a sale of dirhams on credit (riba).
4
Compare with the Maliki view
Imam Malik analogizes qard to other debts. The Hanafi response is that the analogy fails due to the unique nature of qard at inception.
5
Apply the exception for bequests
The text addresses the objection where a bequest of a loan with a term is binding. Explain why the bequest case is different (a donation becomes binding by will).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ہشتم
كتاب البيع

وكل دين حال اذا اجله صاحبه صار مؤجلا لما ذكرنا الا القرض فان تاجيله لا يصح لا نه اعارة وصلة في الابتداء حتى يصح بلفظة الاعارة ولا يملكه من التبرع كالوصى والصبى ومعا وضة في الانتهاء فعلي اعتبار الابتداء لا يلزم التاجيل فيه كما فى الاعارة اذ لا جبر فى التبرع وعلى اعتبار الانتهاء لا يصح لانه يصير بيع الدراهم بالدراهم نسيئة وهو ربو او هذا بخلاف ماذا اوصى ان يقرض من ماله الف درهم فلا تا الى سنة حيث يلزم الورثة من ثلثة ان يقرضوه ولا يطالبوه قبل المدة لانه وصية بالتبرع بمنزلة الوصية بالخدمة والسكنى فيلزم حقا للموصى

ترجمہ اور ہر دین محجل جب صاحب دین مدیون کے لئے میعاد مقرر کر دے تو وہ مو جل کرنا صحیح نہیں ہے اس لیے کہ یہ ابتداء میں اعادہ اور تبرع ہے حتی کہ لفظ اعادہ کے ساتھ صحیح ہو جاتا ہے اور جو شخص تبرع کا مالک نہیں ہوتا وہ اس کا بھی مالک نہ ہو گا جیسے جی اور صہبی اور انتہاء میں معاوضہ ہے پس ابتداء کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے اس میں میعا ولازم نہیں ہوتی ہے جیسا کہ اعادہ میں اس لیے کہ تبرع میں جبر نہیں ہوتا اور انتہاء کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے میعاد صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ دراہم کی بیج دراہم کے عوض ادھار ہو جائے گی حالانکہ یہ ر ہوا ہے اور یہ اس صورت کے برخلاف ہے کہ جب ایک آدمی نے وصیت کی کہ اس کے مال میں سے ایک ہزار درہم فلاں کو ایک سال کے ادھار قرض دیدینا تو ورثہ پر لازم ہوگا کہ اس کے تہائی مال میں سے اس کو قرضہ دیدیں اور مدت سے پہلے اس سے مطالبہ نہ کریں۔ کیونکہ یہ تہر کرنے کی وصیت ہے جیسے غلام کی خدمت اور مکان میں رہنے کی وصیت کی ہو تو یہ موصی کے حق کی وجہ سے لا زم ہوگی ۔ تشریح قرض اور دین میں فرق یہ ہے کہ قرض وہ مال کہلاتا ہے جس کو آدمی اپنے اموال سے جدا کر کے دوسرے کو نفع اٹھانے کے لیے دیدے اور قرضہ لینے والے کے ذمہ میں جو ثابت ہوتا ہے وہ دین ہوتا ہے قرض نہیں ہوتا اور دین وہ کہلاتا ہے جو ذمہ میں واجب ہو خواہ عقد کی وجہ سے خواہ کسی کی کوئی چیز ہلاک کر دینے کی وجہ سے مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ہر وہ دین جس کی ادائیگی فی الحال واجب ہو اگر اس میں صاحب دین مدیون کے واسطے کوئی میعاد معلوم مقرر کر دئے تو یہ دین مؤجل ہو جائے گا۔
دلیل سابق میں گذر چکی کہ دین صاحب دین کا حق ہے وہ اپنے حق کو بالکلیہ ساقط بھی کر سکتا ہے اور مدیون کی سہولت کے پیش نظر دین کے مطالبہ کو مؤخر بھی کر سکتا ہے لیکن ہمارے نزدیک اگر قرض ادا کرنے کے لیے کوئی میعاد مقرر کی گئی تو یہ درست نہیں ہے بلکہ میعاد مقرر کرنے کے بعد قرض خواہ قرضدار سے فوری مطالبہ کر سکتا ہے حاصل یہ کہ اگر قرضہ میں کوئی میعاد مقرر کی گئی تو قرضہ درست ہو جائیگا اور میعاد باطل ہو جائے گی۔ البتہ امام مالک کے نزدیک قرضہ میں میعاد مقرر کرنا، امت سے امام مالک کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ( قرنہ ( قرضدار کے ذمہ میں واجب ہوتا ہے جیسا کہ دوسرے ڈیون واجب ہوتے ہیں جو کہ کس طرح دوسرے دیون میں میعاد مقرر کرنا درست ہے اس طرح قرضہ میں میعاد مقرر کرنا درست ہو گا۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ قرض ابتداء تو اعادہ اور تبرع ہے چنانچہ اگر قرضہ دینے والے نے اقرضتك هذه الالف کی جگہ

اعرتک هذه الالف کہا تو صحیح ہو جاتا ہے اور جو شخص صلات اور تبرعات کا اختیار نہیں رکھتا اس کو قرضہ دینے کا بھی اختیار نہیں ہے جیسے نابالغ بچہ، وصی ، غلام، مکاتب۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ قرضہ تبرعات میں سے ہے اور انتہاء کے اعتبار سے قرضہ،معاوضہ ہے کیونکہ آدمی قرینہ اس لیے دیتا ہے تا کہ اس کے بعد اس کا بدل لے یہی وجہ ہے کہ قرضہ لینے والے پر بعینہ اس چیز کا واپس کرنا واجب نہیں ہوتا جو اس نے قرض خواہ سے لی ہے بلکہ اس کے مثل کا واپس کرنا واجب ہوتا ہے۔ اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ قرضہ انتہاء کے اعتبار سے معاوضہ ہے پس قرضہ کی ابتدائی حالت کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے تو قرض میں میعاد لازم نہ ہوگی جیسا کہ اعادہ کے اندر میعاد لازم نہیں ہوتی چنانچہ اگر کسی نے اپنا کوئی سامان ایک ماہ تک کے لیے عاریت پر دیا تو عاریت پر دینے والا اگر چاہے تو اپنا سامان فوری طور پر واپس لے سکتا ہے کیونکہ عاریت پر دینا تبرع ہے اور تبرعات میں جبر نہیں ہوتا۔ اور میعادا اگر لازم کر دی گئی تو یہ مغیر پر جبر ہوگا کیونکہ میعاد لازم کر دینے کی صوت میں عاریت پر دینے والا میعاد گذرنے سے پہلے مطالبہ کرنے کا مجاز نہ ہوگا پس جس طرح اعادہ میں میعاد لازم نہیں ہوتی اسی طرح قرضہ بھی چونکہ تبرع ہے اس لیے قرضہ میں بھی میعاد لازم نہ ہوگی اور انتہا کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے میعاد مقرر کرنا صحیح نہ ہوگا کیونکہ قرضہ انتہاء کے اعتبار سے میعاد مقرر کرنے کی صورت میں بیج الدراہم بالدراہم نسئیہ ہو جائیگا۔ اور در اہم کے عوض ادھار بیچنار ہوا ہے اگر چہ دراہم میں کی زیادتی نہ ہو اور ربوا کا لازم آنا فساد قرض کا تقاضہ کرتا ہے حالانکہ شریعت اسلام میں قرضہ دینا مندوب اور مستحب ہے اور قرضہ کے جواز پر امت مسلمہ کا اجماع ہے۔ پس ہم نے قرضہ کی ابتدائی حالت کا اعتبار کیا۔ اور بغیر لزوم میعاد کے اس کے جواز کے قائل ہو گئے لیکن یہاں ایک اشکال ہے وہ یہ کہ اگر کسی نے مرض وفات میں یہ وصیت کی کہ میرے مال میں سے ایک ہزار درہم خالد کو ایک سال کے وعدہ پر قرض دید بنا تو یہ قرض مؤجل ہوتا ہے اور اس کی میعاد لازم ہے چنانچہ مرنے والے کے دار تین مرنے والے کے تہائی مال میں سے خالد کو قرضہ دیں گے اور ایک سال تک واپسی کا مطالبہ نہ کریں گے۔ اس سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ قرضہ میں میعاد مقرر کرنے سے میعاد لازم ہو جاتی ہے حالانکہ آپ نے فرمایا ہے کہ میعاد لازم نہیں ہوتی ۔ جواب مرنے والے نے ایک احسان اور تبرع کی وصیت کی ہے اور وصیت میں وہ چیز لازم ہو جاتی ہے جو غیر وصیت میں لازم نہیں ہوتی۔
پس قرضہ دینے کی وصیت میں میعاد مقرر کرنے سے میعاد لازم ہو جائے گی اگر چہ غیر وصیت میں میعادلازم نہیں ہوتی جیسا کہ اگر کسی نے یہ وصیت کی کہ میرا غلام فلاں کی خدمت کرے گا یا فلاں آدمی میرے مرنے کے بعد میرے مکان میں سکونت کریگا تو یہ وصیت کرنے والے کے حق میں لازم ہوگا۔ اور جیسے اگر کسی نے کسی کے لیے اپنے باغ کے پھلوں کی وصیت کی تو یہ وصیت صحیح اور لازم ہے اگر چہ وصیت کے وقت پھل معدوم ہیں اسی طرح قرضہ دینے کی وصیت میں میعاد لازم ہو جائے گی اور میت کے ورثاء کو میعاد مقررہ یعنی ایک سال سے پہلے موصی لڑ سے قرضہ واپس کرنے کے مطالبہ کا حق نہ ہو گا جمیل احمد غفرلہ ۔
Every debt that is due (payable immediately) when its owner defers it becomes deferred, except for a loan (qard). Indeed, its deferment is not valid because it (the loan) is a gratuitous loan and a donation in the beginning, such that it is valid with the wording of 'ariyah (gratuitous loan), and one who does not have ownership of donation (e.g., a guardian or a minor) does not have ownership of it. And at the end it is a compensation. So considering the beginning, deferment is not binding in it, as in 'ariyah, because there is no coercion in donations. And considering the end, it is not valid because it becomes a sale of dirhams for dirhams on credit, and that is riba. This is contrary to when someone bequeaths that from his wealth a thousand dirhams be loaned to so-and-so for one year: then the heirs are obligated from one-third of his wealth to loan it to him, and they cannot demand it before the period, because it is a bequest of donation, like a bequest of service or residence, so it becomes binding as a right of the testator.

Translation: And every debt that is due (payable immediately) when the creditor sets a term for the debtor, it becomes deferred—except for a loan (qard), because its deferment is not valid. This is because a loan is a donation and a beneficence in the beginning, such that it is valid with the word 'ariyah. And a person who does not own charitable acts cannot own it, like a guardian and a minor. And at the end it is a compensation. So considering the beginning, the deferment is not binding in it, as in 'ariyah, because there is no compulsion in charity. And considering the end, the deferment is not valid, because it becomes a sale of dirhams in exchange for dirhams on credit, and that is riba. And this is contrary to the case when a person bequeaths that from his wealth a thousand dirhams be given as a loan to someone for one year: then it is binding on the heirs that from one-third of his wealth they give him the loan, and they cannot demand it before the period. Because this is a bequest of charity, like the bequest of a slave's service or residence in a house, so it becomes binding due to the testator's right.

Clarification: The difference between qard and dayn is that qard is the wealth that a person sets apart from his own wealth to give to another to benefit from, and what is established in the debtor's liability is dayn, not qard. Dayn is what is obligatory in the liability, whether due to a contract or due to destroying someone's property. The issue is that every debt whose payment is immediately obligatory, if the creditor sets a known term for the debtor, then this debt becomes deferred.

Proof: It has passed earlier that debt is the right of the creditor; he can completely drop it, and he can postpone its demand for the debtor's convenience. But in our view, if a term is set for repaying a loan (qard), it is not valid; rather, after setting the term, the creditor can demand immediate payment. The conclusion is that if a term is set in a loan, the loan is valid and the term is void. However, according to Imam Malik, setting a term in a loan is valid. His proof is that a loan becomes obligatory in the debtor's liability like other debts; just as setting a term is valid in other debts, similarly it is valid in a loan. Our proof is that a loan is a donation and beneficence in the beginning; indeed, if the lender says 'I give you this thousand as a gift' instead of 'I give you this thousand as a loan', it is valid. And one who does not have authority over prayers and donations does not have authority to give a loan, like a minor, guardian, slave, or mukatab. Thus it is established that a loan is a form of donation. And considering the end, the loan is a compensation, because a person gives a loan so that afterwards he takes its replacement. That is why the borrower is not obligated to return the exact same item he took, but to return its like. This shows that the loan is a compensation at the end. So considering the initial state, deferment would not be binding in a loan, just as deferment is not binding in 'ariyah. For example, if someone lends his item as 'ariyah for a month, the lender can take it back immediately if he wishes, because 'ariyah is a donation and there is no compulsion in donations. If deferment were made binding, it would be compulsion over the donee, because in the case of binding deferment, the lender would not be permitted to demand it before the term. So just as deferment is not binding in 'ariyah, similarly, since a loan is a donation, deferment is not binding in it. And considering the end, setting a term is not valid because in the case of setting a term, it becomes a sale of dirhams for dirhams on credit. That is selling dirhams on credit for dirhams, even if there is no increase, and the occurrence of riba necessitates the corruption of the loan, while in Islamic law giving a loan is commendable and the ummah has consensus on its permissibility. So we considered the initial state of the loan and deemed it permissible without binding deferment. But there is an objection: if someone in his death illness bequeaths that from his wealth a thousand dirhams be given as a loan to Khalid on a one-year term, then this loan is deferred and its term is binding. Thus, the deceased's heirs from one-third of his wealth will give the loan to Khalid and will not demand repayment for one year. This shows that setting a term in a loan makes the term binding, even though you said it is not binding. Answer: The deceased has made a bequest of a favor and donation, and in a bequest, that which is not binding without a bequest becomes binding. So setting a term in a bequest of a loan makes the term binding, although without a bequest it is not binding. Just as if someone bequeaths that his slave serve so-and-so, or that so-and-so reside in his house after his death, this bequest is binding on the testator's right. Similarly, if someone bequeaths the fruits of his garden to someone, this bequest is valid and binding even though the fruits were non-existent at the time of bequest. Similarly, in the bequest of a loan, the term becomes binding, and the heirs of the deceased have no right to demand repayment of the loan from the legatee before the stipulated term, i.e., one year. Jameel Ahmad, may Allah forgive him.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on Al-Hidayah, explaining the Hanafi ruling regarding the deferment of debts, specifically focusing on loans (qard). The author begins by stating the general principle: any debt that is due immediately becomes deferred if the creditor sets a term. However, this rule excludes loans (qard). The reason is that a loan has a dual nature: at its inception, it is a donation ('ariyah) because the lender gives the money voluntarily without immediate compensation. At its conclusion, it becomes a compensation, as the lender expects repayment of an equivalent amount. The Hanafi school considers the initial donation aspect to be dominant, so setting a binding term would be like forcing a donation, which is impermissible. If the loan is viewed from the end (like a sale on credit), it would involve the sale of dirhams for dirhams on credit, which is riba (interest) and invalid. Thus, the deferment clause is void, and the creditor can demand repayment immediately. The passage then contrasts this with the view of Imam Malik, who permits deferment in loans by analogy to other debts. The Hanafi response is that the analogy fails because the loan is fundamentally a donation at the start, not a pure exchange. The text also addresses a potential objection: if a person bequeaths a loan with a term in his will, then the term is binding. The answer is that a bequest is a special form of donation that creates binding obligations even if the same act without a bequest would not be binding. This is analogous to bequests of service or residence. The commentary emphasizes that the Hanafi position is based on the nature of the loan and avoids riba, while preserving the commendable act of lending. The passage concludes by clarifying that the term is void in a regular loan contract but valid in a bequest.

Key Terms
قرض (qard): A loan of consumable goods (like money) where the borrower is obliged to return an equivalent amount, not the exact item.
دین (dayn): A debt that is obligatory in one's liability, whether from a contract or tort.
عارہ (ariyah): A gratuitous loan of a non-consumable item for use without compensation, where the lender can reclaim it anytime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the stipulation of a term (deferment) void in a loan (qard) contract?
A) Because the Quran explicitly prohibits deferment in loans.
B) Because a loan is a donation at inception, so deferment would coerce a donation, and at conclusion it would become a sale of dirhams on credit involving riba.
C) Because the borrower cannot be trusted to repay on time.
D) Because loans are only valid when given without any conditions.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a loan (qard) cannot be deferred because it is a donation at inception; any deferment term is void, except if the loan is given by bequest.

The Binding of a Fixed Term in a Loan Contract
Ruling on whether a fixed term is binding in a loan and exception in a bequest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a fixed term in a loan is not binding and explain the evidence based on analogy (qiyas) to 'ariyah' and the avoidance of riba.

Core Idea
A fixed repayment term in a loan is not binding; it would transform the loan into a prohibited credit sale of money.

This passage from al-Hidayah tackles a critical issue in loan contracts: can a lender stipulate a binding repayment deadline? The Hanafi school takes a firm position that such a term is invalid because it conflicts with the loan's nature as a charitable act. The commentator explains the reasoning through analogy to a loan for use, and then addresses a tricky objection involving bequests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is stipulating a fixed term (mi'ad) in a loan contract (qard). The ruling: the term is not binding; if stipulated, it is void and ignored.
2
Locate the Dalil
The evidence is analogy (qiyas) to a loan for use (ariyah) and the risk of falling into riba if the term is considered binding from the end.
The proof is aqli (rational), not scriptural.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
Both ariyah and qard are donations (tabarru') at inception; accepting a binding term would impose compulsion on the donor, which is impermissible. From the end, a binding term turns qard into a credit sale of currency (riba).
4
Note the Objection
An objection arises from bequests: if a dying person bequeaths a loan with a term, the term becomes binding. How does this reconcile?
The objector says the analogy fails.
5
Examine the Hanafi Response
A bequest (wasiyyah) is a pure donation subject to the testator's stipulations; it can make binding what is not binding in ordinary contracts. This is an exception, not a counterexample.
The ruling for ordinary loans stands.

فرمایا ہے کہ میعاد لازم نہیں ہوتی ۔ جواب مرنے والے نے ایک احسان اور تبرع کی وصیت کی ہے اور وصیت میں وہ چیز لازم ہو جاتی ہے جو غیر وصیت میں لازم نہیں ہوتی۔

Book of Sales. Ashraf al-Bidaya, Urdu commentary on al-Hidayah. After the announcement of the custom? this statement is correct. And a person who does not have authority over prayers and donations does not have authority to give a loan, such as a minor, an executor, a slave, a mukatib. So it is established that a loan is among donations. And from the perspective of its end, a loan is a compensation, because a person gives a loan in order to later take its substitute. This is why it is not obligatory for the borrower to return the exact same item he took from the lender, but rather to return its like. From this it is known that the loan is a compensation at its end. So, considering the initial state of the loan, a fixed term is not binding in a loan, just as a term is not binding in a loan for use. For if someone lends an item for use for one month, the lender can demand it back immediately if he wishes, because lending for use is a donation, and there is no compulsion in donations. If the term is made binding, it would be compulsion on the lender, because if the term is made binding, the lender would not have the right to demand it back before the term expires. So just as a term is not binding in a loan for use, similarly, since a loan is a donation, a term is not binding in a loan. And considering the end, stipulating a term is not valid, because if the term is considered from the end, it becomes a sale of dirhams for dirhams on credit. And it is a sale of dirhams on credit even if there is no increase in the dirhams, and the necessary occurrence of riba necessitates the invalidity of the loan, whereas in Islamic law giving a loan is recommended and desirable, and the Muslim community has consensus on its permissibility. So we have considered the initial state of the loan and ruled for its permissibility without the bindingness of the term. But here there is an objection: if someone in his deathbed bequeaths that from my wealth one thousand dirhams be given to Khalid as a loan on a promise of one year, then this loan is deferred and its term is binding. So the executors will give Khalid the loan from one-third of the deceased's wealth and will not demand its return for one year. From this it appears that stipulating a term in a loan makes the term binding, whereas you said the term is not binding. Answer: The deceased made a bequest of a favor and a donation, and in a bequest, that which is not binding outside a bequest becomes binding. So in a bequest to give a loan, stipulating a term makes the term binding, even if outside a bequest the term is not binding, just as if someone bequeaths that his slave should serve so-and-so or that so-and-so should reside in his house after his death, this bequest becomes binding for the testator. And just as if someone bequeaths the fruits of his garden to someone, this bequest is valid and binding even if the fruits are nonexistent at the time of bequest. Similarly, in a bequest to give a loan, the term becomes binding, and the heirs of the deceased do not have the right to demand the loan back from the legatee before the specified term of one year.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on whether a fixed repayment term (ajal) is binding in a loan contract (qard). The commentator begins by noting that a loan is fundamentally a charitable act (tabarru'), because the lender parts with his wealth without immediate compensation. Only those who have legal capacity for charity (such as sane adults) can give loans. From the perspective of its termination, however, a loan resembles an exchange (mu'awada) because the borrower must return an equivalent. This dual nature is key. The text then presents the main ruling: when considering the initial charitable nature, a fixed term is not binding in a loan. The analogy is made to a loan for use (ariyah): if you lend an item for a month, you can demand it back at any time because it is a donation. Imposing a binding term would constitute compulsion on the donor, which is impermissible. If a term is considered from the end (i.e., as an exchange), it transforms the loan into a sale of currency on credit (nasiah), which is riba and invalidates the loan. Therefore, to preserve the permissibility of a loan (which is based on consensus), we consider the initial charitable aspect and rule that a fixed term is not binding. An objection is then raised: if a person on his deathbed bequeaths a loan with a one-year term, the term becomes binding; the executors must give the loan and wait for the term. How can this be reconciled with the ruling that a term is not binding? The answer is that in a bequest (wasiyyah), the act itself is a donation and a favor. A bequest can make binding things that are not binding in ordinary contracts, because the testator's donation is subject to his stipulations. Examples are given: bequeathing a slave's service, residence in a house, or future fruits of a garden—all are valid and binding despite being donations. Thus, the binding term in a bequest of a loan is an exception, not the rule. The relied-upon position is that in a normal loan, a fixed term is not binding; if stipulated, it is considered void, and the loan remains valid without it. This is to avoid falling into riba.

Key Terms
قرض (qard): A loan of a fungible item (like money) where the borrower returns an equivalent (mithl) in quantity and quality.
میعاد (mi'ad): A fixed term or deadline for repayment of a loan.
تبرع (tabarru'): A voluntary, gratuitous act or contract without material compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what happens if a person on his deathbed bequeaths a loan with a one-year term?
A) The term is not binding, just like a normal loan without a will.
B) The term becomes binding because the bequest is a donation and can make stipulations binding.
C) The loan is invalid because it constitutes a sale of currency on credit.
D) The loan must be repaid immediately despite the will.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a fixed repayment term in a loan is not binding; the loan remains valid but the term is void to avoid riba.

Definition and Prohibition of Riba
Introduces the chapter on riba, its linguistic and legal definition, and its prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define riba linguistically and legally, state its prohibition as haram, and list the five Quranic punishments and the Prophetic curse.

Core Idea
Riba is any increase without countervalue in an exchange; it is haram by definitive evidence with severe divine punishments.

This lesson opens the chapter on riba, a central prohibition in Islamic finance. The commentator defines riba both linguistically and legally, then anchors its severity with explicit Quranic verses and a Prophetic curse. Understanding this foundation is essential for recognizing prohibited transactions.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definitions
Identify the linguistic meaning (increase) and the legal definition (increase without countervalue in a property exchange).
The legal definition is specific to exchange contracts.
2
State the ruling
Note that riba is haram by ijma and textually prohibited.
Haram level is confirmed by Quran and Sunnah.
3
List the five Quranic punishments
Enumerate the punishments from the verses: confusion, destruction, war, disbelief, eternal Hell.
These highlight the severity.
4
Note the Prophetic curse
The hadith curses the consumer, provider, witnesses, and scribe of riba.
This further emphasizes prohibition.
5
Understand chapter placement
Riba chapter comes after permissible sales (e.g., murabahah) because prohibition follows command, and both involve increase but differ in permissibility.
Helps grasp the logical sequence of the book.

Chapter of Riba. This chapter is about the explanation of riba. Book of Sales. Explanation: riba, with kasra on the ra', linguistically means increase. So it is said: this exceeds (yarbo) this. Allah Almighty says: 'And whatever you give for increase (riba) to increase in people's wealth, it does not increase with Allah.' And a high place is called rabwah for the same reason: it is higher and exceeds all other places. In legal terminology, riba is the name for a financial increase in an exchange of property for property for which there is no countervalue. Riba is forbidden by consensus and its prohibition is textually established. Allah Almighty says: 'And Allah has forbidden riba.' 'O you who believe, do not consume riba.' And Allah Almighty has stated five punishments for the consumer of riba: (1) Confusion: His saying: 'They will not stand except as one whom Satan has confounded.' (2) Destruction: His saying: 'Allah destroys riba.' (3) War: Allah Almighty said: 'Then be informed of a war from Allah and His Messenger.' (4) Disbelief: Allah Almighty said: 'And leave what remains of riba if you are believers.' (5) Eternal in Hell: His saying: 'And whoever returns, those are the companions of the Fire, they will abide therein.' And the Prophet cursed the consumer of riba, the one who provides it, its two witnesses, and its scribe. There is a hadith in Tirmidhi from Ibn Mas'ud: 'The Prophet cursed the consumer of riba, the one who provides it, its witnesses, and its scribe.' The connection between this chapter and previous chapters is that the previous chapters dealt with sales that Allah commanded, while this chapter deals with sales that Allah prohibited. Because prohibition comes after command, the chapter on riba is placed after the chapters on commanded sales. And the chapter on riba has a special connection with the chapter on murabahah: in both there is an increase, but the increase in murabahah is halal and the increase in riba is haram. Since the original state of things is the basis, the rulings of murabahah were mentioned first and the rulings of riba later.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on riba (usury/interest). The commentator begins with a linguistic note: riba (ربا) means increase, from the root ر ب و. The legal definition is narrower: it is an increase in value received without any countervalue in an exchange of property for property (particularly money and food items). Riba is universally forbidden (haram) by consensus of the Muslim community, and its prohibition is explicitly from the Quran and Sunnah. The passage then lists five punishments mentioned in the revelation for those who engage in riba: (1) Resurrection in a state of madness/confusion; (2) destruction of wealth; (3) declaration of war from Allah and His Messenger; (4) if one insists on consuming riba after prohibition, it amounts to disbelief; (5) eternal punishment in Hell for those who return to it. A hadith from Tirmidhi is cited: the Prophet cursed the consumer, the provider, the witnesses, and the scribe of riba. The commentator then explains the placement of this chapter: previous chapters (like murabahah) dealt with permissible or recommended sales; this chapter deals with prohibited sales. Because the prohibition (nahy) follows the command (amr), the chapter on riba comes later. The connection with murabahah is that both involve an increase, but in murabahah the increase is legitimate profit, while in riba it is unjust. The ordering principle is that the original state (permissibility) is addressed first, then exceptions (prohibition). This passage is foundational for understanding the severe gravity of riba in Islamic law.

Key Terms
ربوا (riba): An increase without countervalue in an exchange of property for property, specifically in money and food items.
معاوضہ (mu'awada): An exchange of property for property with expectation of equivalence.
حرام (haram): Absolutely forbidden; committing it is a sin and incurs punishment in this world and the Hereafter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many punishments for riba are listed in the Quran according to this passage?
A) Three: destruction, war, and confusion.
B) Five: confusion, destruction, war, disbelief, and eternity in Hell.
C) Seven: including curse of the Prophet and punishment in the grave.
D) Four: destruction, war, disbelief, and curse.

Key Takeaway
Riba is forbidden by definitive evidence, and its definition includes any increase without countervalue in an exchange of property for property, with five specific Quranic punishments and a Prophetic curse.

Definition and Prohibition of Riba in the Hanafi School
Introduces the chapter on riba, defines it, and states its prohibition with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of riba, explain its prohibition with Quranic evidence, and list the five punishments mentioned for the consumer of riba.

Core Idea
Riba is any excess without countervalue in a wealth exchange, strictly prohibited by Quran and hadith.

This lesson introduces the chapter on riba from Ashraf al-Bidayah, a classical Hanafi commentary on al-Hidayah. The passage begins by defining riba linguistically and legally, then establishes its absolute prohibition with a series of Quranic verses and a hadith, detailing five severe punishments. Understanding this foundational ruling is essential for all transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic root of riba
The passage explains that riba means increase, citing the verse from Surah ar-Rum and the term rabiwah for high ground.
Linguistic meaning sets the foundation for the legal definition.
2
Extract the legal definition
The legal definition: an increase in wealth in exchange of wealth without a corresponding countervalue. Note that the element of 'without countervalue' is key.
This definition is broader than just loans; it includes commodity exchanges.
3
Locate the evidence for prohibition
The passage quotes Quranic verses (al-Baqarah 275-279) and a hadith from Tirmidhi. Identify each verse's specific punishment.
The prohibition is explicit and categorical.
4
Enumerate the five punishments
List the five punishments: insanity on Judgment Day, annihilation of wealth, war from Allah and His Messenger, disbelief for those who persist, and eternal hellfire.
These highlight the severity of riba.
5
Understand the chapter arrangement
The passage explains why the riba chapter comes after chapters on lawful sales and murabahah.
Arrangement reflects fiqh methodology: command before prohibition.

Ashraf al-Bidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah volume 8. Page 289. Chapter of Riba. This chapter is about the explanation of riba. Explanation: Riba linguistically, with kasrah of ra', means increase. Thus it is said, 'This exceeds this.' Allah says, 'And whatever you give in riba that it may increase in the wealth of people, it does not increase with Allah.' A raised place is called rabiwah because it is higher than all other places. In Shari'ah terminology, riba is the name for an increase in wealth in exchange of wealth for which there is no countervalue in return. Riba in its entirety is haram and its prohibition is scriptural. Allah says, 'And Allah has forbidden riba.' O believers, do not consume riba. And Allah Almighty has mentioned five punishments for the consumer of riba: (1) Insanity: His saying, 'They do not arise except as one whom Satan has confounded by touch.' i.e., on the Day of Judgment, the riba consumer will not arise except like a person whom Satan has made mentally deranged. (2) Annihilation: His saying, 'Allah destroys riba.' (3) War: Allah said, 'Then be informed of war from Allah and His Messenger.' i.e., give news of war to those who consume riba. (4) Disbelief: Allah said, 'And leave what remains of riba if you are believers.' i.e., if after the prohibition of riba you still consider it halal, you exit faith and enter disbelief. (5) Eternal abode in the Fire: Allah's saying, 'And whoever returns, then those are the companions of the Fire, they will abide therein forever.' The Messenger cursed the eater of riba, its writer, and its two witnesses. As in Tirmidhi from Ibn Mas'ud: 'The Messenger cursed the eater of riba, its agent, its witnesses, and its writer.' The connection between this chapter and previous chapters is that previous chapters discussed those sales which Allah commanded to perform, and in this chapter those sales are mentioned which Allah prohibited. Since prohibition comes after command, the chapter of riba is placed after previous chapters. And the chapter of riba has specific connection with the chapter of murabahah because in both there is increase, but the increase in murabahah is halal and the increase in riba is haram. And because the original state of things is permissibility, the rulings of murabahah are mentioned first and the rulings of riba after.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the chapter on riba (usury/interest). It begins with a linguistic definition of riba as 'increase', citing the Quranic verse (Surah ar-Rum: 39) and the etymology of rabiwah (raised ground). The legal definition follows: any increase in a wealth-for-wealth exchange without a corresponding countervalue. The prohibition is stated as categorical (haram), supported by the explicit Quranic verse (Surah al-Baqarah: 275). The author then enumerates five severe punishments for the one who consumes riba: (1) arising like a madman on Judgment Day (Surah al-Baqarah: 275), (2) the wiping out of wealth (Surah al-Baqarah: 276), (3) war from Allah and His Messenger (Surah al-Baqarah: 279), (4) leading to disbelief (Surah al-Baqarah: 278), and (5) eternal hellfire for those who persist (Surah al-Baqarah: 275). The hadith from Ibn Mas'ud (recorded in Tirmidhi) curses not only the consumer but also the writer and witnesses of riba, showing the comprehensive prohibition. Finally, the passage explains the arrangement of chapters: the command to engage in trade (verse: 'And seek from the bounty of Allah') comes before the prohibition of riba, so the chapter on riba follows the chapters on lawful sales. Additionally, the chapter on murabahah (sale at cost-plus) is placed before riba because both involve increase, but murabahah's increase is lawful while riba's is unlawful, and the default permissibility takes precedence.

Key Terms
ربوا (ribā): In Shari'ah, riba is an increase in wealth in an exchange of wealth for wealth without a corresponding countervalue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Riba is defined as an increase without countervalue in wealth-for-wealth exchanges, and its absolute prohibition is established by explicit Quranic verses and hadith.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Riba al-Fadl: Ruling and Effective Cause
Ruling on riba al-fadl with proof and effective cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on riba al-fadl, identify the effective cause, and apply it to new scenarios.

Core Idea
Exchanging like makyool or mawzoon items with excess is riba – the 'illa is measurement or weight plus same kind.

Understanding the foundational prohibition of riba is essential for every Muslim. This lesson unpacks the classical Hanafi position on riba al-fadl (excess riba), drawing on the authoritative matn of Imam al-Quduri and the commentary of al-Hidayah. You will learn not only the ruling but the underlying effective cause that makes it applicable to all makyool and mawzoon items. The proof is the famous hadith of the six commodities, narrated by multiple companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is selling a makyool or mawzoon item with its own kind with an excess in quantity. The ruling in the Hanafi school is haram (prohibited).
Riba al-fadl involves excess; riba al-nasi'ah involves delay; both are prohibited.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith of the six items (gold, silver, wheat, barley, dates, salt) narrated by Abu Sa'id al-Khudri and others. The key phrase: 'like for like, hand to hand, and any excess is riba.'
The hadith is mutawatir (mass-transmitted) according to the Hanafis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Hanafi school infers the effective cause ('illa) from the common characteristic of the six items: they are customarily sold by weight or volume. Therefore, the 'illa is kail (volume measurement) or wazn (weight) combined with unity of kind (jins).
Thus, any item that is makyool or mawzoon falls under the ruling.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam al-Shafi'i limits the 'illa to the six specific items, arguing that the hadith cannot be extended by qiyas. The Hanafi school disagrees, generalizing the 'illa to all makyool/mawzoon items.
This difference affects many transactions, e.g., rice, meat, and other commodities.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, if you exchange 2 kg of wheat for 1 kg of wheat, it is riba because it is the same kind and makyool, with excess. To avoid riba, sell one for cash and then buy the other, or ensure equal weight/volume and immediate delivery.
Different kinds (e.g., wheat for barley) are not subject to riba al-fadl, but may involve riba al-nasi'ah if deferred.

Book of Sales. Page 290. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah volume. The sale of a makyool or mawzoon item with its own kind with excess is riba. His statement: Imam al-Quduri said: 'Riba is prohibited in every makyool or mawzoon when sold with its kind with excess. So the effective cause according to us is measurement with kind or weight with kind.' It is said: 'measurement with kind' and this includes both. The origin in this chapter is the famous hadith: 'Wheat for wheat, like for like, hand to hand, and any excess is riba.' He enumerated six items: wheat, barley, dates, salt, gold, silver on this example. And it is narrated with two narrations: with raf' (nominative) 'mithlun' and with nasb (accusative) 'mithlan'. The meaning of the first is 'sell the dates' and the second is 'sell dates'. Translation: Imam al-Quduri said: Riba is prohibited in every makyool or mawzoon item when it is sold with its kind with excess. So the effective cause according to us is measurement with kind or weight with kind. The author of al-Hidayah says that 'measurement with kind' is also said, and it includes both. The basis in this chapter is this famous hadith: 'Wheat for wheat, like for like, hand to hand, and the excess is riba.' The Messenger enumerated six items on this example: wheat, dates, gold, barley, salt, silver. And this hadith is narrated with two narrations: with raf' 'mithlun' and with nasb 'mithlan'. The meaning of the first is 'sale of dates' and the second is 'you sell dates'. Explanation: The author of Quduri says that riba is prohibited in every makyool and mawzoon item, provided it is sold with its kind with excess or deficit. So according to us, the effective cause of riba is measurement with kind or weight with kind, meaning that the two items exchanged are both makyool or both mawzoon and their kind is one. And if both countervalues are mawzoon but kind is not one, like gold and silver, or both are makyool but kind not one, like wheat and barley, then between them riba and excess is not prohibited. The author of al-Hidayah said that the word 'measurement' because it includes both measurement and weight, so it is also said that the effective cause of riba is measurement with kind. The basis for measurement with kind as the effective cause is the famous hadith. This hadith is narrated by sixteen companions. The author of al-Hidayah mentioned a portion of the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: the complete hadith is: 'Gold for gold, like for like, hand to hand, and any excess is riba; silver for silver, like for like, hand to hand, and any excess is riba; wheat for wheat, like for like, hand to hand, and any excess is riba; barley for barley, like for like, hand to hand, and any excess is riba; salt for salt, like for like, hand to hand, and any excess is riba; dates for dates, like for like, hand to hand, and any excess is riba.' The hadith of 'Ubadah ibn al-Samit: He said: The Messenger said: 'Gold for gold...'
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents the core ruling on riba al-fadl (excess riba) from the Hanafi madhhab. The matn of Imam al-Quduri states: 'Riba is prohibited in every makyool (measured by volume) or mawzoon (weighed) item when sold with its own kind with excess.' The effective cause ('illa) for the prohibition according to the Hanafi school is the combination of measurement (kail) or weight (wazn) with unity of kind (jins). In other words, whenever two items of the same commodity type are exchanged, and those items are customarily sold by volume or by weight, any excess in quantity over an equal exchange constitutes riba. The commentary from al-Hidayah clarifies that the term 'qadr' (quantity) can encompass both kail and wazn, and thus the 'illa can be expressed as 'qadr ma'a al-jins' (quantity with same kind). The proof is the famous hadith of the six commodities (gold, silver, wheat, barley, dates, salt), which the Prophet (peace be upon him) specified, saying 'like for like, hand to hand, and any excess is riba.' The hadith is narrated by many companions (sixteen are mentioned), establishing its strength. The passage also explains that if the items are of different kinds (e.g., wheat and barley, gold and silver), riba al-fadl does not apply; only riba al-nasi'ah (deferment) may apply if credit is involved. The two narrations of the hadith, one with 'mithlun' (nominative) and one with 'mithlan' (accusative), do not affect the legal meaning: both emphasize equality. Thus, the Hanafi ruling is that any exchange of like makyool or mawzoon items must be equal in quantity and immediate (hand-to-hand) to avoid riba.

Key Terms
مكيل (makīl): An item conventionally sold by volume measure (e.g., grains, liquids).
موزون (mawzūn): An item conventionally sold by weight (e.g., gold, silver, meat).
العلة (al-ʻillah): In this context, the effective cause that triggers the prohibition of riba: combination of measurement or weight with unity of kind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Being a specific enumerated item
B) Kail or wazn with unity of kind
C) Being a food item
D) Hand-to-hand delivery

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits riba al-fadl in all makyool/mawzoon items of the same kind due to the shared 'illa of measurement/weight with kind, as evidenced by the hadith of the six items.

Riba in Makiil and Mawzun: The Hanafi Position and Hadith Proof
Ruling with proof from hadith and linguistic analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on riba al-fadl for makiil and mawzun items, identify the 'illa (effective cause) as kail/wazn with jins, and apply the hadith proof to determine whether a given exchange involves prohibited riba.

Core Idea
Riba al-fadl is prohibited in all same-genus makiil/mawzun items because the 'illa is quantity (kail/wazn) with unity of jins.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi school's position on riba al-fadl (excess riba) in sales of measured and weighed items. The core ruling is that any excess in quantity when exchanging two items of the same genus that are both sold by volume or weight is haram. This ruling is based on the famous hadith of the six items and extended via qiyas using the 'illa of measurement/weight combined with genus. We will work through the textual proof, the grammatical analysis of the hadith, and the derivation of the 'illa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the sale of a makiil or mawzun item with its own kind with excess. According to Imam al-Quduri, such a sale involves riba and is haram. The ruling level is haram (prohibited).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The primary dalil is the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri and 'Ubada ibn al-Samit, which lists six items (gold, silver, wheat, barley, dates, salt) and commands that they be exchanged like for like, hand to hand, and declares any excess as riba.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is qat'i (definitive) regarding the prohibition of excess.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly prohibits excess in each of the six items. The Hanafi school identifies the 'illa as kail (measurement) or wazn (weight) combined with unity of genus (jins). This is derived from the fact that the six items are either makiil (wheat, barley, dates, salt) or mawzun (gold, silver). The word 'qadr' (quantity) is also used to encompass both.
The 'illa is not limited to the six items; it extends to all items that are makiil or mawzun.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
The Shafi'i school disagrees, limiting the 'illa to foodstuffs (tu'm) and currencies (thaman). Thus, they do not extend riba to non-food mawzun items like iron. The Hanbali school agrees with the Hanafis but includes additional items.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view within the school, based on the general wording of the hadith and the qiyas from the six items to all makiil/mawzun items of the same genus. This is the position in Zahir al-Riwayah.
The authority weight is zahir_riwayah.

كتاب البيوع
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ہی والفضة بالفضة والبر بالبر والشعير بالشعير والتمر بالتمر والملح بالملح مثلاً بمثل سواء بسواء يداً بيد فاذا اختلفت هذه الاصناف فبيعوا كيف شئتم اذا كان يداً بيد یہ حدیث اعراب کے اعتبار سے دو طریقہ پر مردی ہے ایک تو کلمات ستہ اور لفظ مثل کے رفع کے ساتھ اس صورت میں مضاف مقدر ہوگا تقدیری عبارت یوں ہوگی بیع التمر بالتمر یا بیع الذہب بالذہب مثل بمثل بیع التمر مبتدا ہوگا اور مثل اس کی خبر ہو گی دوسری روایت میں یہ کلمات اور لفظ مثل منصوب ہیں۔ اس صورت میں فعل امر بیجو ا مقدر ہوگا تقدیری عبارت یوں ہوگی بیعو التمر الحدیث یہ کلمات بیوا کا مفعول ہونے کی وجہ سے منصوب ہوں گے ۔ اور لفظ مثلاً حال ہونے کی وجہ سے منصوب ہو گا حدیث میں مثلاً بمثل سے مراد یہ ہے کہ دونوں عوض کیل یا وزن میں برابر ہوں اور ید ابید سے مراد یہ ہے کہ دونوں عوضوں پر مجلس عقد میں قبضہ ہو جائے حاصل یہ کہ اتحاد قدر اور اتحاد جنس کی صورت میں عوضین میں کیل یا وزن کے اعتبار سے برابر بھی شرط ہے اور عوضین پر قبضہ کرنا بھی شرط ہے۔
Book of Sales. 290. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary on al-Hidayah, volume. The sale of a makiil or mawzun item with its own kind with excess becomes riba. The ruling: Riba is prohibited in every makiil or mawzun item when sold with its own kind with excess. The 'illa (effective cause) according to us is kail (measurement) with genus (jins) or weight (wazn) with genus. He said: And it is said 'qadr (quantity) with genus' and this encompasses both. The origin in this chapter is the famous hadith: his statement (peace be upon him): 'Wheat for wheat, like for like, hand to hand, and the excess is riba.' He enumerated the six items: wheat, barley, dates, salt, gold, silver on this example. And it is narrated in two narrations: with raf' (nominative) 'mithl' and with nasb (accusative) 'mithlan'. The meaning of the first is 'the sale of dates' and the meaning of the second is 'sell dates'. Commentary: Imam al-Quduri said: Riba is prohibited in every makiil and mawzun item, provided it is sold with its own kind with excess. So according to us, the 'illa of riba is kail with genus or wazn with genus. The author of al-Hidayah says: It is also said 'qadr with genus' and this encompasses both. The origin in this chapter is the famous hadith: 'Wheat for wheat, like for like, hand to hand, and the excess is riba.' The Prophet (peace be upon him) enumerated six items on this example: wheat, barley, dates, salt, gold, silver. And this hadith is narrated with two riwayat: with raf' on 'mithl' and with nasb on 'mithlan'. The meaning of the first is 'the sale of dates' (as a subject) and the meaning of the second is 'sell dates' (as a verb). Explanation: Imam al-Quduri says: Riba is prohibited in every makiil or mawzun item, provided it is sold with its own kind with excess. So according to us, the 'illa of riba is kail with genus or wazn with genus, meaning that the two items being exchanged are both makiil or both mawzun, and their genus is the same. If both items are mawzun but genus differs (like gold and silver) or both makiil but genus differs (like wheat and barley), then riba and excess are not prohibited. The author of al-Hidayah said: The word 'qadr' encompasses both kail and wazn, so it is also said that the 'illa of riba is qadr with genus. The origin for qadr with genus being the 'illa of riba is the famous hadith. This hadith is narrated by sixteen Companions. The author of al-Hidayah mentioned a portion of the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri. The complete hadith is: from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Gold for gold, like for like, hand to hand, and the excess is riba; silver for silver, like for like, hand to hand, and the excess is riba; wheat for wheat, like for like, hand to hand, and the excess is riba; barley for barley, like for like, hand to hand, and the excess is riba; salt for salt, like for like, hand to hand, and the excess is riba; dates for dates, like for like, hand to hand, and the excess is riba.' The hadith of 'Ubada ibn al-Samit: He said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Gold for gold, silver for silver, wheat for wheat, barley for barley, dates for dates, salt for salt, like for like, equal for equal, hand to hand. If these categories differ, then sell as you wish provided it is hand to hand.' This hadith is narrated in two ways with regard to grammatical analysis: one with the six words and the word 'mithl' in the nominative case; in this case there is an elided noun, the intended phrase is 'the sale of dates for dates' or 'the sale of gold for gold, like for like' – 'the sale of dates' is the subject and 'mithl' is the predicate. In the second narration, these words and the word 'mithl' are accusative; in this case there is an elided imperative verb 'sell', the intended phrase is 'sell dates – the hadith' – these words are accusative because they are the object of 'sell', and the word 'mithlan' is accusative as a circumstantial adverb (haal). In the hadith, 'mithlan bi mithlin' means that the two compensations are equal in measurement or weight, and 'yadan bi yadin' means that possession of both compensations takes place in the contract session. The conclusion is that when there is unity of qadr and unity of genus, equality in measurement or weight is a condition, and taking possession of the compensations is also a condition.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof from the hadith, explaining the Hanafi position on riba in makiil (measured by volume/capacity) and mawzun (weighed) items. The author first quotes Imam al-Quduri's authoritative statement that riba is prohibited in every makiil or mawzun item when sold with its own kind with excess. The effective cause ('illa) according to the Hanafi school is kail (measurement) together with same genus (jins), or weight together with same genus. The author of al-Hidayah adds that the word qadr (quantity) can be used to encompass both kail and wazn, and that this 'illa is derived from the famous hadith of the six items narrated by numerous Companions. The passage then provides the full text of the hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri and 'Ubada ibn al-Samit, emphasizing that the hadith requires both equality in measure/weight and hand-to-hand transfer (spot transaction). The grammatical analysis of the hadith is given: the two narrations differ in whether the key word mithl/mithlan is nominative or accusative, affecting the interpretation—one implies a verbal noun construction (the sale is like for like), the other an imperative (sell like for like). The core ruling is that for two items of the same genus and same category (both makiil or both mawzun), any excess is riba and prohibited, and both equality and immediate possession are conditions. If the genus differs (e.g., wheat and barley) or one is makiil and the other mawzun, riba does not apply in excess, but hand-to-hand transfer may still be required by other rules.

Key Terms
ربا (riba): Excess or increase in exchange without equivalent consideration, prohibited in Islamic law.
مکيل (makiil): An item sold by volume (kayl), e.g., wheat, barley, dates, salt.
موزون (mawzun): An item sold by weight (wazn), e.g., gold, silver, iron.
علة ('illa): The effective cause or ratio legis that connects the ruling to its underlying reason, used in qiyas.
جنس (jins): Genus or kind; same genus means items of the same type (e.g., wheat with wheat).
مساواة (musawa): Equality in quantity (kayl or wazn) between the two items exchanged.
قبضه (qabdah): Taking possession; required to be in the contract session (hand to hand) for ribawi transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the 'illa (effective cause) for riba al-fadl according to the Hanafi school?
A) The item being a foodstuff (tu'm)
B) Being makiil or mawzun with same genus
C) The item being a currency (thaman)
D) The sale being on credit (nasi'ah)

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, riba al-fadl is prohibited in any exchange of same-genus makiil or mawzun items if there is excess in quantity, based on the hadith of the six items and the 'illa of quantity with genus.
